        BURKINA FASO EIGHTH POVERTY REDUCTION SUPPORT CREDIT

                         CREDIT AND PROGRAM SUMMARY

Recipient:                Burkina Faso

Implementing Agencies:    The Ministry of Economy and Finance (MEF) will be responsible for overall
                          implementation of the proposed PRSC-8, in close association with several
                          line Ministries.

Amount:                   SDR 61.5 million (US$100 million equivalent)

Terms:                    On IDA credit terms with a forty year maturity including a grace period of ten
                          years.

Schedule of disbursement: Single tranche of SDR61.5 million (US$100 million equivalent) to be
                          available upon effectiveness.

Description:              The proposed PRSC-8 is the second of a third series of PRSCs in support of
                          Burkina Faso's second Poverty Reduction Strategy (PRS) adopted in 2004.
                          The PRSC-8 aims to support the acceleration of real growth and the reduction
                          of poverty incidence in Burkina Faso by supporting action to: (i) improve the
                          investment climate and promote exports; (ii) improve access to basic services
                          through deepened decentralization; and (iii) promote efficiency, transparency
                          and accountability in the use of public resources through enhanced public
                          financial management.

Benefits:                 The measures supported by the proposed PRSC-8 are expected to have a
                          significant positive direct impact on poverty reduction.       Specifically, the
                          improvement in the investment climate and the promotion of exports
                          supported by the proposed operation would sustain economic growth. The
                          reforms in public service provision through decentralization would improve
                          access of the poor to education, health, water and sanitation services, support
                          their greater participation in the growth process and sustain progress towards
                          the MDGs. The strengthening of public financial management is expected to
                          enhance efficiency, transparency and accountability in public resources use.
                          Such measures would also increase the country's absorption capacity for
                          external resources.

Risks:                    Several risks could jeopardize the expected outcomes and benefits of this
                          operation. First, as Burkina Faso continues to be vulnerable to commodity
                          price fluctuation, further increases in oil and food prices could undermine
                          macroeconomic stability and progress made in poverty reduction. High oil
                          prices drain international reserves and increase petrochemical, electricity and
                          transportation costs with adverse impact on the overall economy. Increased
                          world prices on basic food commodities translate into higher inflation and
                          hurt the poor, particularly in urban centers.     Should the macroeconomic
                          outlook deteriorate, social tensions could affect political stability, in the run
                          up to the presidential elections in 2010.      Also, lower cotton prices on
                          international markets weaken the country's economic and fiscal performance

                       and negatively affect cotton producers' incomes. To address these risks, the
                       Government is implementing a three-pronged approach, including: (i)
                       implementing a cotton price smoothing fund to help stabilize profits and
                       losses over time, reducing uncertainties on future income for producers in a
                       given year; (ii) in the medium term, lowering energy costs by connecting with
                       lower-cost regional producers and opening the electricity and petroleum
                       sectors to private management and investment; and (iii) over time, supporting
                       economic diversification through reform of the investment climate and
                      promotion of mining and irrigated agriculture. With respect to the food price
                       increases, as indicated in Box 1, the Government has taken a series of
                      measures, including a temporary lifting of the import tax on products of mass
                       consumption and measures to relaunch the 2008/09 agricultural campaign
                      through seed distribution. The Government is accompanied in these reform
                      efforts by the series of PRSCs and other IDA operations, and support from a
                      range of other development partners. The World Bank is also assisting with a
                      US$5 million nutrition project for small children and pregnant women
                       approved by the Board on June 5,2008.

                       Second, as a Sahelian economy, Burkina Faso is highly vulnerable to
                      weather risk and climate change.           The current cotton campaign and
                       subsistence agriculture has been negatively affected by late rains. Efforts to
                       improve competitiveness of the sector are hurt by reduced output and
                       farmers' rising indebtedness. As part of the ongoing Country Economic
                       Memorandum, the World Bank is exploring the feasibility of developing
                      market instruments to transfer weather risk.

                      Third, Burkina Faso's overall competitiveness is affected by the strong
                      appreciation of the Euro against the US dollar, as the CFAF is pegged to
                      the Euro. This is negatively affecting revenue of cotton producers in CFAF
                      terms and revenue mobilization for the budget, while real appreciation of the
                      domestic currency further hampers the economy's diversification by loss of
                      comparative advantage. To partly hedge against this risk, the World Bank
                      will be providing technical assistance to SOFITEX to develop a risk
                      management strategy based on market instruments and traditional techniques.

                      Fourth, the implementation of the decentralization strategy poses
                      significant challenges. Officials working at the center may resist the transfer
                      of responsibilities, while local Governments may not have sufficient
                      resources and capacity to carry out their new responsibilities. To address
                      these challenges, the Government has adopted a participatory and gradual
                      decentralization approach that should ensure stakeholder consensus, capacity
                      building at the local level and fiscal sustainability over time. Several donors
                      support decentralization in Burkina.         This PRSC series accompanies
                      decentralization reforms on sectors which progress faster (education, water
                      and sanitation) and match delegation in competencies with genuine financial
                      transfers from the center.

Operation ID Number:  PO99011




                                            vii


                                     I. INTRODUCTION

1.      This program document proposes a single-tranche Eighth Poverty Reduction
Support Credit (PRSC-8) for SDR 61.5 million (US$100 million equivalent) to Burkina
Faso. The reform program supported by the proposed PRSC8 builds on Burkina Faso's PRSP-I1
and is aligned with the Bank's Africa Action Plan (AAP) and the June 2005 Burkina Faso
Country Assistance Strategy (CAS). It is the second in a third series of three such operations,
covering the period 2007-2009, to support the implementation of selected reforms aimed at
acceleratingreal growth and reducing the incidence of poverty in Burkina Faso.

2.      Continued progress in PRSP implementation since 2004 has been demonstrated by
three Annual Progress Reports (APR) and was made possible through sustained growth and
moderate inflation, structural reforms in the cotton sector, progress in the business climate and
investments linked to the realization of the MDGs. The 2006 Annual Progress Report (APR),
which was circulated to the Board for information on April 20, 2008 together with the related
IMF-World Bank Joint Staff Advisory Note, reconfirms the Government's continued
implementation of the PRS, in combination with its new medium term economic framework
(MTEF), and extends the Government's strategy from 2008 to 2009.

3.      The proposed PRSC-8 aims to further support the acceleration of real growth and
the reduction of poverty incidence in Burkina Faso by supporting actions to: (i) improve the
investment climate and promote exports; (ii) improve access to basic services through deepened
decentralization; and (iii) promote efficiency, transparency and accountability in the use of
public resources through enhanced public financial management. Specifically, PRSC-8 would
help improve the competitiveness of the cotton sector (particularlythrough the revision the price
setting mechanism for domestic producers to be better aligned with world market price trends
and efforts to reduce SOFITEX'S operating costs and launch its operational audits for 2004-
2007), support the Extractive Industries Transparency Initiative, improve the business climate by
facilitating land titles and construction permit delivery, improve labor market effectiveness and
facilitate the adoption of a key law on energy regulation. PRSC-8 would also support the
decentralization of basic services and accompanying deconcentration of budget management
structures with the adoption of key texts in the basic education, health and water and sanitation
sectors, as a preliminary step towards the actual transfer of financial resources to local
governments for the management of infrastructure under their area of responsibility. Lastly,
PRSC-8 would help strengthen the planning capacity of the Ministry of Infrastructure and the
Ministry of Environment, support implementation steps in the public procurements reform, and
strengthen the independence and the efficiency of the Audit Court responsible for external
controls.

4.      The proposed size of PRSC-8 (US$100 million equivalent) is larger than initially
envisaged for this operation (US$70 million). This increase responds to Burkina's exceptional
budgetary financing needs in 2008, which have resulted from the combined impact of another
crisis in the cotton sector (a poor crop year due to late rains) and of worldwide hikes in food and
oil prices, affectingparticularly the poorest segment of the population. The Governmenthas put
in place a sound emergency package to respond to the crisis in the short run, which involves a
combination of food distribution through existing food banks, VAT exemptions on basic

foodstuff to assist the poor, and subsidies on agricultural inputs including fertilizer for cotton
farmers and staple food seeds for subsistence farmers. In parallel a new impetus is being given
to longer term measures to boost agricultural productivity, in particular through an ambitious
green revolutionprogram.

5.      Bilateral and multilateral donor assistance will continue to play an important role in
supporting the implementation of PRSP-11, given that the country's own resources fall short
of the levels required for achieving the objectives spelled out in the strategy. The Second PRSP
created the momentum for a significant group of donors to align their budgetary support under a
common framework, the General Budget Support Framework (Cadre Gdndral dJOrganisation
des Appuis Budgdtaires CGAB), providing an harmonized framework for policy dialogue and
decisions linked to progress in the implementation of PRSP 11. In this context, PRSC-8 was
prepared jointly and in complementarity with the African Development Bank's (AfDB) latest
Poverty Reduction Strategy Support Project (PASRP-4).


                                11. COUNTRY CONTEXT



6.      Despite severe exogenous shocks, the country was able to maintain macroeconomic
stability. Economic growth continued to slow down from 5.5 percent in 2006 to 4.0 percent in
2007 due to a fall in cotton production and adverse terms of trade shocks. This slowdown
combined with a sharp increase in food prices and overall inflation, breeding discontent and
causing riots over the high cost of living in some cities between February and May 2008 (see
Box 1). This performance contrasts with the preceding decade when Burkina Faso's real GDP
growth averaged 5.9 percent, significantly above the average for the WAEMU, thanks to the
pursuit of structural reforms and of steady investment, and in a context of low inflation. Lower
growth rates in 2006 and 2007 are mainly due to slowed agricultural production, particularly a
decline in cotton production and exports. As a result of the food and energy price increase, 12-
month inflationreached 7.7 percent in April 2008 and is expected to average 6.2 percent in 2008,
compared to 2.4 percent in 2006 and -0.2 percent in 2007. The Government has taken a series of
measures to mitigate the impact of these price increases on the poorest, including a temporary
liftingthe import tax on products of mass consumptionand seed distribution (see details in Box 1
and Annex 4).

                        Box 1: Burkina Faso's Response to the Food Prices Crisis


The recent increase in food prices bred discontent and caused riots and strikes over the high cost of living in some
cities between February and May 2008. 12-month inflation reached 7.7 percent in April 2008 and is expected to
average 6.2 percent in 2008, compared to 2.4 percent in 2006 and -0.2 percent in 2007. Surges in the prices for food and
energy products were the main driver of the recent price increase. The increase in prices of specific food commodities
started in mid-2007, following a short rainy season and severe floods in 20 provinces, and has been quite substantial over a
 12-month period, reaching up to 62 percent for shea oil (used for cooking in rural areas), 55 percent for white maize, and
50 percent for cotton oil. The prices of other heavily consumed cereals increased by 28 percent for sorghum and 12-14
percent for rice. Over the same period, the prices for salt and milk increased by 45 percent and 22 percent, respectively.
The authorities are now faced with commodity price volatility at both the national and the international levels. This
development, whose impact is likely to remain over time, constrains the Government's capacity to implement its poverty
reduction policies and reduce households' vulnerability.

In order to mitigate the impact of the price increases in the short term, in late 2007 the Government temporarily
lifted the import tax on products of mass consumption such as rice, salt, pasta, sweet concentrated milk, and infant
milk.    The transmission of the import duty relief to consumers will depend on the pass-thru rate (see Annex 4).
Preliminary estimates suggest that full transmission of the tax break to consumers would erase or even reverse the recent
surges in prices of five out of the six products affected by the measure. This policy measure will have more impact among
the urban poor, who tend to be more directly affected by the price crisis. The elimination for six months of customs duties
on rice, salt, pasta and milk products is estimated to cost around CFAF 12 billions to the budget or 0.3 percent of GDP. In
addition, the authorities put 5,000 tons of cereals on the market to be sold at a social price. A cornit6 de veille has defined
price ceilings in order to guarantee food security (CFAF 11,000 for a bag of kg 100 of maize and sorghum, CFAF 11,500
for a bag of small millet).

Government's efforts also include activities to boost agricultural production in the short term, which are being
supported by donors. FA0 and the AfDB are supporting projects to boost subsistence farmers' production and reduce
the country's dependency on imported rice. Similarly, the World Bank group is supporting agricultural output in 2008
together with compensation schemes for farmers' losses sustained. The Community-Based Rural Development Project
provided, at the launch of the 2008 agricultural campaign, 3,500 tons of improved millet, sorghum, maize, beans, and rice
seeds to 140,000households in 302 communities in the country for a total cost of $5 million. The targeting of households
was done by the village councils, taking into account the level of poverty and the impact of flooding. Early planting was
encouraged to take advantage of the rains. Further discussions with the Government are taking place for additional
financing to purchase more seeds and to support the rice production in the dry season which starts in October 2008.
Efforts to diversify agriculture, including by IFC and private sector participation, are being pursued. Finally, a new
project supporting a longer term Government strategy for food security is being prepared for launch in FY09.

The government is also launching an ambitious green revolution as a longer-term response to boost domestic food
production, employment, household revenue and exports. The objective of Burkina's Green Revolution is to complete
by 2015 the development of a market-integrated agriculture and competitive agro-industrial products resulting from
sustainable improvement of agro-sylvo-pastoral productivity through diversification, intensification and higher production
targets in selected high-value added goods which are believed to have a significant impact on food security and GDP
growth. The effort will benefit vegetable productions, including traditional cereals (sorghum, maize), rice, cowpeas
(niibk), fruits and vegetables (tomato, onion, mango), fishery productions (fish and derived products), livestock resources
(cattle, pork and poultry), and forestry (combustion wood, rubber and resin). To achieve the production targets, the
strategy proposes to: (i) design highly productive, resilient and high value-added varieties; (ii) use soil fertilization,
defense and restoration management techniques; (iii) promote agricultural mechanization; (iv) use preservation and
protection techniques for stocked vegetables and commodities; (v) develop and use production-related irrigation,
transformation and rehabilitation techniques; (vi) strengthen producers' operational capacity by promoting special credit
operations and increasing mechanical/technical training opportunities; (vii) produce seeds that are certified for both the dry
and wet seasons, based on each agricultural regions' specific needs.

7.       The 2007/08 cotton campaign has proven to be a limp year, while gold is becoming
Burkina's second export commodity. Cotton and cash crop production have sharply decreased
owing mainly to bad weather but also to a fall of 14 percent in cotton producer prices in CFAF;
price increases of inputs; delays in payments to farmers for the previous season's crop; and
finally,the late announcement of minimum producer prices. This situation led to a sharp decline
in cotton farmers' revenues and an increased indebtedness of cotton farmers' cooperatives (see
Box 2). The profitability of the cotton sector and the competitiveness of Burkina's exports
beyond cotton were also negatively affected by the cumulative appreciation of the real effective
exchange rate of 16 percent since 1999. Burkina Faso's terms-of-trade have only marginally
recovered since they plummeted in 2005, with the appreciation of the Euro and higher cotton
prices offsetting the surge in oil prices. These trends were partially offset by rising mining
exports in 2007. Gold is Burkina's principal mineral resource and the 1996 liberalization of the
sector attracted several foreign investors. Mining production grew from 7.4 to 9 tons between
2006 and 2007, strengthened by the active exploitation of the Taparko gold mines and improved
organizationof gold panning.

                             Box 2: Burkina Faso's Cotton Crisis and Recovery

The substantive reforms undertaken in the cotton sector (the adjustment of the price mechanism and the
recapitalizationof cotton companies) have improved profitability for the sector as a whole. The new price mechanism
adopted by the interprofession improved profitability prospects for the sector as a whole, given international cotton prices.
Indeed, the cotton farm gate floor price was lowered from CFAF165 per kilo in 2006-2007 to CFAF145 per kilo in 2007-
2008, to adjust the sector to declining world prices. This is a much lower floor price than was adopted in C8te d'Ivoire
(CFAFISO), Mali (CFAF165) and Benin (CFAF185) during the same period, demonstrating a consensus among
stakeholders to bring domestic prices in line with international prices, rather than shield producers from a decline in world
prices. At the same time, the recapitalization of SOFITEX by the Government to cover cumulated losses of the three prior
cotton campaigns before the domestic price mechanism was adjusted was completed in December 2007 for US$46 million
(or 0.7 percent of GDP).

Despite these reforms, the 2007108 cotton campaign has proven to be a limp year. Cotton and cash crop production
have sharply decreased owing mainly to bad weather but also to a fall of 14 percent in cotton producer prices; price
increases of inputs; delays in payments to farmers for the previous season's crop; and finally, the late announcement of
minimum producer prices. Farmers reduced the acreage given over to cotton and expanded planting of cereal crops, such
as maize, millet and sorghum. Farmers have only produced 434,000 tons of seed-cotton this past campaign, down from
649,400 tons a year earlier, with an average yield of SOOkglha, significantly below historical averages.

These developments could negatively affect the next cotton campaign unless the government intervenes. The poor
crop season has increased indebtedness among cotton producers associations and between those associations and financial
institutions. The problem was further aggravated by a late mobilization by cotton companies of the campaign credit needed
to pay producers for the seed cotton they supplied in November. Payment delays have driven some producers to sell their
assets to survive and have induced capital losses. Internal indebtedness has spread among producers through the local
solidarity mechanism known as caution solidaire. Consequently, even the most productive farmers have experienced a
shortfall in revenue, having to forgo their profit to cover the other members' debts, putting many of them in a difficult
financial position to acquire seeds and fertilizers as the 2008109 campaign started.         To mitigate the impact of this
decapitalization in the sector, the government intends to use part of the proceeds from the proposed PRSC to finance a
rescheduling package for: (i) domestic arrears accumulated by village cooperatives toward the banking system and cotton
companies; and (ii) the resolution of the cooperatives internal debt.

The cotton crisis has been further exacerbated by soaring fertilizer prices. The surge in input prices has become a
major binding constraint for the development of the cotton sector. The Burkinabe government has, since 2002, given the
equivalent of CFAF 3 billion per annum in subsidies for inputs. But since input prices have increased sharply in 2007
(from 10 to 30 percent), this is further aggravating the financial position of producers, many of whom are in debt,
preventing them from producing cotton again. To mitigate this risk, the Government intends to use some of this PRSC
proceeds to finance an increase of fertilizers subsidy from CFAF 3 billion to CFAF 6.5 billion.

The new cotton price smoothing fund, whose prime objective is to balance out volatility in international cotton prices for
the benefit of domestic cotton farmers, is likely to mitigate the impact of the current crisis on the next cotton campaign,
providing a boost to many discouraged cotton producers. Producers received in May a price complement of CFAF IOIkg,
since world prices rose in 2007108 significantly above their start-of-campaign projection levels. This "ristourne" for
producers has constituted a positive incentive to continue cotton production.        The smoothing fund is a farmer-ginner
operated instrument to be financed initially by a concessional credit from AFD on-lent to the Fund by the Government, and
by payments from the ginners linked to the world price increase experienced over the campaign period. The smoothing
fund, however, is not currently sufficiently provisioned to effectively work over the medium term. In addition, a regional
orland national line of credit planned to be a key feature for the price smoothing fund sustainability still needs to be set up.

The outlook for cotton is favorable on world markets. With the demand for cotton surging and as China continues to
absorb a large share, cotton prices are forecast to stay strong through the coming year. In a June 2, 2008 update, the
International Cotton Advisory Council (ICAC), an association of cotton growing and consuming countries, forecasted a
season-average Cotlook A Index of 79 cents per pound in 2008109, a 6 percent increase from 2007108. An expected
significant decline in the stocks-to-mill use ratio in the World-less-China (Mainland) is pushing prices higher in 2008109.
Production is expected to increase slightly in mainland China and India, remain stable in Pakistan, and decline further in the
United States. Demand during the current cotton year is forecast to rise by 7.0%, which is likely to continue to push world
prices up. These developments could help cotton production in Burkina Faso rebound during the 2008109 campaign.

8.      Substantial and higher than expected inflow of foreign aid, increasingly on a grant
basis, helped contain the current account deficit. The current account deficit widened in 2007
to 13.1percent of GDP (from 12.6percent in 2006). Cotton exports fell in 2007, partly offset by
an increase in gold exports. Imports of products that are becoming more expensive (energy and
food) represented a larger share of total imports. Burkina Faso's 2007 balance of payments
deficit was financed by external financial assistanceand, for a growing part, by private flows and
transfers. Burkina Faso is now receiving half of the World Bank and African Development Bank
financing in the form of grants due to the reclassification of its debt distress risk. Gross
international reserves have continued to increase to reach nearly 7 months of imports in 2007,
above the standardreserve adequacy measures or WAMU requirements.

9.      Fiscal policy has been broadly in line with the targets set under the authorities'
program, as confirmed by the successful completion of the second review under the IMF
PRGF Arrangement on June 30, 2008. Despite a rise in public expenditure partly due to
exogenous shocks and the limited domestic resource mobilization, the fiscal deficit (excluding
grants and on a cash basis) was 11.6 percent of GDP, 2 percentage points less than the program
target. Total revenue represented 13.6 percent of GDP in 2007, roughly unchanged from 2006.
Current revenue reached 13.6 percent of GDP in 2007, a 0.6 point of GDP increase from 2006,
but still below the present WAEMU community norm of 17 percent. Expenditure represented
25.7 percent of GDP in 2007, as against 24.3 percent in 2006. Expenditures were constrained by
the need to raise salaries as agreed with trade unions, repair the damage caused by the flooding in
the Centre-Est Region in June 2007, a substantial subsidy to SONABEL, the national electricity
company, because of the leap in oil prices, and by SOFITEX'Srecapitalization (US$46 million in
2007, or 0.7 percent of GDP) partly refinanced from the proceeds of the successful sale of 51
percent of ONATEL, the telecommunication company. Despite these expenditure pressures, the
Government was able to increase poverty reducing expenditures by 10 percent, from CFAF 167
billion in 2006 to about CFAF 183 billion in 2007, of which education represented roughly
CFAF 77 billion and health CFAF 60 billion.

10.     External debt indicators have worsened in 2007, mainly because of the depreciation of
the US dollar and the fall of exports resulting from the drop in cotton production. The net
present value of external debt represented 118 percent of exports in 2007 as compared to 97
percent in 2006. The country has relatively strong controls for the contracting of external debt,
however, as was assessed by the Debt Management Performance Assessment (DEMPA) mission
in May 2008. In particular, the authorities, which have recently adopted a national debt policy,
strictly enforce the PRGF 35 percent concessionality floor for all central-government foreign-
currency borrowings, and consistently scrutinize projects through a National Public Debt
Committee before contracting foreign debt. A debt strategy that should be added as an annex to
the 2009 budget is being finalized, which would include a ceiling on new borrowings based on a
recent debt sustainability analysis.



11.     Real GDP growth is projected to reach 4.5 percent in 2008 and average 6 percent in
the medium run, which is broadly in line with the historical average of the past ten years.
Surging oil and food prices will result in an increase of annual average inflation to above 6
percent, but the IMF expects inflation to abate in 2009-2010 to its historical average of about 2

percent as international food and oil prices stabilize (at a higher level). Moreover, weather
conditions more aligned with historical averages and supply responses to government's policies
designed to relaunch domestic food production are likely to ease inflationary pressure from 2009
onward. The outlook for cotton is favorable on world markets (Box 2) and several mining
projects are under way which will help gold exports from commercial mines grow significantly.
The exploitation of the Mana and Kasaka gold mines that were started in 2007 should contribute
to increase mining production further by over 10 percent over 2008109. At the sectoral level,
growth is projected to come from a moderate expansion (4-6 percent p.a.) of cotton and
agriculture and through an above average growth of the industry and service sectors (above 8
percent p.a.). Such a performance in the cotton sector is predicated on improvements in the
financial viability of Burkina's three cotton marketing companies and normal rainfall. Growth
will be further driven by a significant expansion of extractive industries (with new mines in
Youga, Taparka and Kalsaka expected to reach exploitation phase by 2009-lo), construction for
transport infrastructure and energy (with the expected link to the C8te d'Ivoire grid). Growth in
the service sector is expected to come from telecommunications,trade and transport.

12.       The authorities intend to continue their effort of budgetary discipline, while
pursuing efforts towards the Millennium Development Goals. The overall fiscal deficit
(excluding grants) should regularly decrease to reach 12 percent of GDP in 2008 and to decline
gradually to around 10percent of GDP in 2010. To further improve performance in tax revenue,
with the goal of reaching the WAEMU tax revenue target of 17 percent of GDP, the authorities
are engaged with IMF support in a program to reform tax and customs administration, and
comprehensive tax policy reforms are planned for the coming years. Key measures going
forward are: (i) the full computerization of the large and medium taxpayer offices, especially for
monitoring taxpayer obligations, and (ii) the simplification of exemptions to make tax
administration more productive and lower compliance and administrative costs. However, the
drop in cotton production could lower tax revenue in nominal terms, which will be partly offset
by higher income tax payments from mining companies. To mitigate this, the authorities have
lowered the corporate income tax, in order to promote private sector development, a measure
which might only reach its objective in the medium term.

13.       The high prices of energy pose a significant risk for the country's growth outlook
and fiscal performance. The country depends on oil and gas for 70 percent of its required
supplies. Import of fuel is a monopoly in the hands of SONABHY, with structuralreforms in the
sector progressing slowly. The IMF estimates that incomplete pass-through of rising petroleum
prices would lead to quasi-fiscal losses of SONABHY, equivalent to 1 percent of GDP'. The
situation for SONABEL is also delicate. While the company has been able to remain financially
viable so far (a rare exception in the region), it would not be able to absorb large cost increases
(due to oil prices) without also increasing its revenues. Electricity prices were gradually
increased, by nearly 24 percent on a cumulative basis, between 2004 and 2006, but further hikes
on electricity prices would affect households' well being. By September 2009, electricity
production costs will be reduced as the power transmission line from Bobo-Dioulasso to
Ouagadougou is completed, bringing to the capital city electricity power imported from C8te
d'Ivoire produced at a lower cost. This would establish the conditions for a progressive

' Burkina Faso - Second Review Under the Three-Year Arrangement Under the Poverty Reduction and Growth
Facility, EBS/08/71, June 13, 2008.

reduction of subsidies in the sector and enable subsidies to be more targeted at the poor and
isolated regions of the country. Beyond 2009, increased participation of the private sector in the
energy sector would contribute to increase productivity. Efforts should also be pursued to
accelerate the construction of other regional interconnections with neighboring countries such as
Ghana and Nigeria to further reduce electricity production costs.

14.     The current account deficit is expected to deteriorate further in 2008 to 16.6 percent
of GDP as higher oil prices are expected to lead to a significant increase in imports. This is
expected to be partially reversed once the connection of the transmission grid with C8te d'Ivoire
is completed, which should reduce the consumption of oil for electricity generation. The
exports-to-GDP ratio will decline significantly in 2008 because of a poor cotton harvest in 2007;
in the medium term, gold exports due to the opening of new commercial mining operations will
support export growth. The current account deficit is expected to return to lower levels in 2009
and 2010, as the tighter fiscal stance dampens import demand. Part of the financing gap will be
financed by the US Millennium Challenge Account that has committed US$0.5 billion in grants
to be disbursed over 5 years to finance poverty reducing and growth enhancing projects.
Overall, grants are projected to account for a sizeable 6.6 percent of GDP in 2008. Over the
medium-term, a fiscal tightening linked to reforms implemented on revenue collection combined
to budget expenses stabilisation around 24 percent of GDP, would have most impact in
narrowingthe country's current account deficit to more sustainable levels.

15.     Official Development Assistance (ODA) will amount to nearly 10 percent of GDP in
2008. Foreign budget support is estimated to provide around CFAF120 billion in 2008. Around
85 percent of it is covered by members of the budget support donor group (Cadre Ge'ne'ral
d'organisation des Appuis Budgktaires     - CGAB), including the African Development Bank,
Denmark, France, Germany, the Netherlands, Sweden and the European Union, together with
IDA. Another CFAF245 billion is expected to be financed through project aid.

16.     The June 2008 joint IMF-World Bank Debt Sustainability Analysis (DSA)
concluded that Burkina Faso's risk of debt distress is high on account of the NPV of debt-to-
exports ratio, while all other debt indicators are comfortably below their policy-dependent
thresholds under the baseline scenario (Annex 6). The NPV debt-to-export ratio breaches the
150 percent threshold in the baseline scenario (relevant for moderately performing countries),
while a year earlier the corresponding ratio remained below 200 percent (the relevant threshold
for highly performing countries). Overall deterioration from the previous DSA suggests that the
authorities need to pursue a prudent fiscal policy, and ensure external financing at the highest
possible concessional level, and a continued build-up in public debt management capacity.


 111. BURKINA FASO'S POVERTY REDUCTION STRATEGY (PRSP-11)

17.     In 2004, Burkina Faso launched its second Poverty Reduction Strategy Paper
(PRSP-11) covering the period 2004-2006. Government has decided to maintain the current
strategy until 2010, providing yearly updates on the PRS Priority Action Plan (PAP). The IMF-
World Bank Joint Staff Advisory Note (JSAN) on the third Progress Report (APR), transmitted
to IDA'S Executive Board on April 20, 2008, confirms the Government's continued satisfactory
implementation of the PRSP-I1 and provides feedback on the strategic objectives targeted in the
latest PAP which covers the period 2008-2010.

18.     Burkina Faso's PRSP-I1 is guided by the stated objective of maintaining a real GDP
growth above 6 percent per annum and halving poverty by 2015. The strategy introduces a
regional dimension and establishes a results-based monitoring and evaluation framework. PRSP-
I1 updates the strategy laid out in the first 2003 PRSP with a focus on accelerated shared growth
across four strategic pillars: (i) improving the competitiveness of the economy (private sector
development), (ii) improving public service delivery and access, (iii) enhancing employment and
income opportunities-especially for the poor, and (iv) accelerating decentralization and
strengthening the efficiency of the public financialmanagement system.

19.    As a result of broadened consultations, PRSP-I1 also expands the number of priority
sectors from the initial three (health, education, and rural development) to twelve, adding: food
security, access to potable water, HIVIAIDS, social inclusion, gender, environment,
employment, informal sector, development of SMEs and SMIs, security, rural electrificationand
Information and Communication Technology (ICT). Since the PRSP-I1 is considered a 'living
document', the agenda moving forward is focused on creating an enabling environment for
accelerated shared growth, clarifying the role of Government in the economy, and the reduction
of regional disparities. In particular, the Government is articulating its development agenda
around the adoption of a road map to achieve the country's green revolution by 2015. This
agenda focuses on the development of a market-integrated agriculture and competitive agro-
industrial products through diversification, intensification and increase output in high-value
added goods with significant impact on food security and GDP growth (vegetables, fishing
production, livestock resources and forestry).

20.    The latest JSAN acknowledges the assessment made in the PRSP-I1 of the country's
key achievements and the identification of priority policies that can foster growth and
reduce poverty. The JSAN also highlights the need for additional efforts to mobilize public
revenue and enhance expenditure analysis and management to increase pro-poor spending. But
more could have been envisaged to remove obstacles to the implementation of the Poverty
Reduction Strategy (PRS) and to translate those actions into a monitorable set of results. Also,
additional measures to further improve the investment climate and strengthen governance are
needed to accelerate private sector growth and employment creation. Increasing spending on
basic services, enhancing the management and efficiency of public expenditure in health and
education, improving basic infrastructure and continued progress on decentralization are critical
to achieve the PRS public service delivery targets; hence the importance of improving the
capacity to monitor and evaluate the PRS implementation.


 IV. BANK GROUP'S SUPPORT TO THE GOVERNMENT'S PROGRAM

A. LINKTO THE COUNTRYASSISTANCESTRATEGY

21.    The latest CAS sets out the Bank Group's support to the Government's PRS for the years
FY06-FY09. The CAS is fully aligned with the PRSP-I1 and contributes to specific country
outcomes such as: (i) expanded and diversified export earnings; (ii) an improved investment
climate; (iii) efficient economic infrastructure; (iv) improved access to quality basic education,
health and HIVIAIDS services; (v) increased access to potable water and sanitation services; (vi)
enhanced labor market functioning; and (vii) improved public resource management in a

decentralized context. It foresees the use of three types of instruments: development policy
operations (PRSCs), investment credits, and analytical and advisory activities (AAA). The
proposed PRSC-8 is an integral part of the CAS as it is designed to provide financing for the
budget and support the implementation of policy reforms in key areas supported by the CAS,
namely to: (i) improve the investment climate and promote exports; (ii) improve access to basic
services; and (iii) promote efficiency, transparency and accountability in the use of public
resources.



22.     The government made significantprogress during 2004-07 (the implementationperiod of
the second PRSC series including PRSC 4, 5 and 6) in its efforts to: (i) improve the investment
climate and promote exports; (ii) improve access to basic services through deepened
decentralization; and (iii) promote efficiency, transparency and accountability in the use of
public resources through enhanced public financial management. Progress made and key results
achieved are detailed further below.

Competitiveness,Export Diversificationand Investment Climate

23.     Macroeconomic Performance. Although Burkina Faso's development endeavors were
adversely affected by a series of external shocks, during 2004-07 the government maintained a
stable macroeconomic environment thanks to solid fiscal discipline, sound policy
implementation and significant inflows of external support.2 Although the terms of trade
deteriorated (by 9 percent between 2004 and 2006), and despite high oil and low cotton prices,
the current account deficit improved as a result of booming cotton exports and high domestic
producer prices for cotton. The fiscal deficit was largely covered by foreign aid. Burkina Faso
has successfullypassed the reviews of the PRGF arrangement since 2004. The Heavily Indebted
Poor Countries Initiative (HIPC) and the Multilateral Debt Relief initiative3 (MDRI) have
rendered debt more sustainable for a given country performance category and freed fiscal space
for priority expenditures. However, only limited progress was made in the mobilization of fiscal
revenueswhich constrained the fiscal space available for poverty reduction spending.

24.     Cotton Sector. SOFITEX no longer holds the monopoly in the sector4and now faces
competition from two private operators: SOCOMA and FASO COTON (15 percent of the
market). Producers and ginning companies created a system for self-governance in 2005, and a
producer price mechanism was set in 2007 to better align domestic prices with world market
prices. Despite this progress, the cotton sector continues to face difficulties due to factors both
internal (high operational costs of ginning operations) and external (low prices and the
appreciation of the CFAF vis-a-vis the US dollar). This points to the need to sustain ongoing
reforms in the cotton sector to improve the financial viability of the cotton companies as well as
improve on-farm productivity, ginningefficiency and product quality.

25.    Economic Diversification. In order to diversify agricultural production and facilitate
exports, the Government: (i) formulated a rural development strategy on crop production


 About 9 percent of GDP.
 Granted in 2006.
4Government's share was kept at 35 percent after SOFITEX's recapitalization

diversification;(ii) consolidated texts harmonizing the execution principles of authority transfers
to regional organizations and private sector; (iii) adopted standards for oilseed products and
enacted a law to facilitate seed trade and technology transfer; and (iv) adopted a restructuring
plan for the Ministry of Agriculture, Water and Fisheries, in accordance with the institutional
audit. Land access and tenure security were enhanced, thereby improving production conditions
for small holders. Sorghum, millet, corn, and cowpea (nibbb) productions have increased by 30
to 60 percent since 2004. In most cases, the increase in production was in fact achieved thanks
to the extension of the cultivation area. However, the expected diversification of exports did not
materialize as the cotton share in total export revenues increased from 48 percent to 60 percent
and exports stagnated at about 9 percent of GDP over the last decade. This highlighted the
remaining challenges related to poor infrastructure and high cost of public services and utilities,
an inadequate policy and institutional framework for private investment and export promotion.

26.     Telecommunications. The sector has been liberalized (with several mobile private
operators flourishing) and the incumbent telecommunications operator (ONATEL) was
privatized in December 2006 (51 percent sold to Maroc T616com for CFAF 144 billion). The
Telecom regulatory body (ARTEL) was set-up in 1998 to regulate the sector. The IDA-funded
Competitiveness and Enterprise Development Project (CEDP) is currently strengthening
ARTEL's capacity. Consequent to the liberalizationof the telecommunications sector (2001), the
number of cellular phone subscriptions increased to one million5in 2006 and the communication
costs between landlines and cellular lines decreased by more than 30 percent6between 2004 and
2006. An independent agency regulates the telecommunicationsector, the reform of which led to
the creation of the Ministry for Postal Services, Information Technology, and of Communication
(MPTIC). The Government also requested expression of interests from the private sector for
participation in the rural telephone service.

27.    Energy.       In line with the measures supported under PRSC-47 and PRSC-s', the
Government prepared a reform strategy in the electricity and petroleum sectors to improve
management, increase supply and reduce production costs; and adopted a legal and regulatory
framework to introduce private sector participation and bring additional production capacity.
Private sector participation was also sought for SONABHY (the national oil company), however
progress was slow in this process, including in terms of preparing accounting and financialaudits
due to funding difficulties.

28.    Although Burkina Faso adjusted its electricity tariffs by 10 percent in 2004 and by 12.5
percent in 2006, they still remain the highest in the region. In 2006, the fuel subsidy mechanism
was reformed to cap the total annual subsidy, and to create an incentive for SONABEL (the
power utility) to search for more effective thermal power generation. The Government has been
able to maintain SONABEL's financial viability thanks to the utility's good operational
performance. Also, SONABEL's disciplined approach to investment planning allowed it to
expand access without causing power shortages.

29.       Investment Climate and Labor Market Reform. To support private sector
development and improve the Doing Business indicators, the authorities:(i) created two Business
Registration Centers (CEFORE~, see paragraph 87); (ii) reduced business registration
formalities; (iii) strengthened the real estate administration in urban areas; (iv) reformed the
urban code in 2006; and (v) improved the legal framework for urban construction work. The
Government also continued to implement the National Action Plan for the Justice Reform. As a
consequence,the costs and timeline in starting a business have been significantly reduced".

30.       Institutions for trade and business facilitation were strengthened; Trade point" and
FASANORM'~were both selected by the CEDEAO for best practices in terms of electronic
trade development; the Burkina Faso Entrepreneurs' Center was made operational; and a
Competitiveness Committee, composed of public and private sector representatives, was
established to make recommendations on and monitor progress in competitiveness. Also, a new
labor law was adopted (2004) and a comparative study on labor market laws and regulations,
which serves as an input for discussions on reform options, was completed (2006).

Poverty Alleviation and Delivery of Basic Sewices

31.       Poverty has decreased over the medium term, but with significant geographic
heterogeneity. The proportion of the population living below the poverty line went down from
46.4 percent in 2003 to 43.0 percent in 2005 and 42.6 percent in 2007. However, growth
dividends are not homogenous among households and depend in great part on their geographic
localization. Areas which are most remote from economic centers did not benefit from growth as
much as others. Also, poverty remains a rural phenomenon. More than 90 percent of poor
people live in the countryside and this proportion is growing over time: 92.2 percent in 2003, 93
percent in 2005, and 94.1 percent in 2007.

32.       However, poverty incidence estimated in 2007 suggests a possible slight
deterioration from 2006. Authorities' analysis of poverty'3 identifies a slight increase in the
incidence of poverty between 2006 and 2007 of 0.5 point and a more significant increase of 0.9
point in rural areas. Moreover, the depth of poverty would have recorded an increase of 0.2 point
and the severity an increase of 0.3 point. These indicators suggest that the minimum amount
necessary for the poor to make up the resource gap compared to the poverty line has increased.
This situation is explained by a drop in average real income by 1.1 percent in 2007, due to the
fall in incomes in the cotton sector, flooding, cereal deficits in some provinces and energy price
rises. As indicated above, the recent food price increases risk further eroding the gains made in
poverty reduction in past years.

33.      Burkina has made satisfactory progress on households' access to basic service^.'^
Indicators show improvement in basic education and health. However, access to basic services
remains challenging. A new Education Act aiming at introducing free schooling up to the age of
 16 was passed. As the Ten-Year Basic Education Development Plan progresses in
implementation, with responsibilities for the hiring and management of school staff transferred
to the regions and the introduction of credit delegation to lower secondary education, the gross
enrollment rates in schools have increased from 41.7 percent in 1999-2000 to 67.9 percent in
2007. The enrolment rate in secondary schools has improved from 19.1 percent in 2005 to 25.9
percent in 2007. These progresses also reflect a regular increase in education expenditures as a
share of GDP from 2.6 to 2.9 percent between 2004 and 2007, even though sectoral investments
have slow down. The Government has used these additional resources to subsidize schooling in
the 20 provinces with the lowest enrollment rates, expand teacher training, and upgrade schools
with running water and sanitation facilities. Overall access to drinking water has improved to 79
percent in 2007 from 54 percent in 2004 with more modest gains in rural areas and Burkina is in
the position to meet the MDG in this sector, as implementation of a roadmap and action plan
progresses.15

34.      Health services have become more accessible on average thanks to a regular increase
in public health expenditures as a share of overall budget (from 7.4 in 2004 to 9.4 percent in
200616),a revision of the fee structure and service contract system for hospitals, the delegation of
credits to district managers, the transfer of more resources to municipalities, enhanced
community participation in the decision-making process on the issue of revolving drug funds,
efforts to attract more health personnel to under-served areas, a focus on childlyouth
malnutrition, distribution of free vitamin A supplements, subsidies for obstetrical emergency
care and delivery services, and gratuity established for all preventive services at the primary
health care level. Vaccination rates remained high,17 resulting in marked improvements in
fighting contagious diseases with a reduction in the mortality rate for meningitis from 21 percent
in 2005 to 8.5 percent in 2006, while a strategic framework for halting the spread of HIVIAIDS
was put in place and implemented, contributing to reduce prevalence from 7.17 percent in 1997
to 2.03 percent in 2006.

35.     However, the country is unlikely to reach several of the MDGs by 2015. Significant
challenges remain as Burkina Faso Human Development Index (HDI)'s ranking indicates:" (i)
the literacy rate is still below 30 percent (28.7 percent in 2007) and the quality of education has
stagnated; (ii) the gender gaps remain in school enrollment rates, literacy rates, primary
completion rates and access to health service, even though they are decreasing; (iii) Burkina
compares less favorably than other countries for assisted births, despite a policy focus on malaria
care for pregnant women and subsidies for obstetrical services, and (iv) mortality figures remain
alaming with very high infant and juvenile mortality, respectively at 81 per 1,000 and 184 per
1,000 in 2003 (it is estimated that 50 percent of deaths in children under the age of five are due
to malnutrition, as 19 percent and 39 percent of children under the age of five suffer from,
