Public Financial Management and Governance

36.     Budget management system. Burkina Faso has made significant progress in improving
its budget management system as evidenced by: (i) the introduction of a global MTEF based on
program budgeting (PRSC-4); (ii) the broadening of the MTEF to key sector ministries such as
health and education (PRSC-4 to 6); (iii) the introduction of a computerized expenditure
management system (CID) capturing all domestically financed expenditures (PRSC-4 to 6), that
of a revenue management system (CIR) (PRSC-4) and an accounting system (PRSC-5; CIE, and
CICL for the regions); (iv) the extension -to  ministries (PRSC-4) and to regions (PRSC-6)- of
the software that combines payroll management (MFB) with personnel management; (v) the
preparation of an inventory of government assets (PRSC-4 and 5); (vi) the establishment of a
computerizedsystem (SYGADE) to monitor external aid flows and to integrate such flows in the
budget information system (PRSC-5 and 6); (vii) improved budget reporting and auditing
through the preparation of timely reports and strengthening of the Audit Court; and (viii)
capacity building in public financial administrations.

37.     Decentralization and deconcentration.         The Government has taken measures to
advance decentralization of public service delivery and deconcentration of its administration to
support local governments. As such, the authorities adopted the decentralization law in 2004,
held the first full local elections in 2006, created a legal framework and introduced related
administrativedecrees, and empowered the communes through transfers of functions, assets and
resources. Progress also included the deconcentration of the payment authority, the progressive
establishment of regional civil service offices, and the extension to sector ministries of the
computerized civil service administration information system (SIGASPE, PRSC-4). However,
and although practical implementation of the decentralizationlaw was envisaged on a pilot basis,
visible progress on decentralization has been slow due to resistance by vested interests, local
governments' capacity constraints and the need to put in place a complex legal and institutional
mechanism for the transfer of powers and resources.

38.     Procurement. During 2004-07, the Government nominated a procurement reform
committee with public sector and civil society representatives to enhance transparency and
scrutiny; adopted a decree regulating concessions and leasing in line with best international
practice; developed a computer application for the monitoring of procurement processes; and
adopted a regulatory text for the issuance and withdrawal of accreditation of public works
(PRSC-5). It also adopted a procurement reform action plan based on the 2005 CPAR
recommendations (PRSC-6). In addition, procurement contracts have been regularly audited by
the Inspection Ge'ne'raledes Finances (IGF).

39.     Corruption. The authorities adopted a national policy and action plan for good
governance, established a High Authority to Combat Corruption, reorganized and expanded the
judiciary, and adopted a national policy to fight corruption in 2006. They also disseminated a
good governance plan.      Despite these efforts, the country's ranking on the Transparency
International's Corruption Perception Index declined by 9 points to the 105th position (out of

 179 countries) in 2007. Burkina Faso remains nonetheless among the better-ranked African
countries. To scale up the country's fight against corruption, the Parliament created the High
Authority for State Oversight (Autorite'Supirieure de ContrGlede 1'Etat, ASCE).

40.     Environment. PRSC-4 and 5 promoted the increase in resources for improving the
assessmentof the impact of Government activitieson the environment.

C. COORDINATION       WITH OTHER   DEVELOPMENT     AGENCIES   AND THE IMF


41.     PRSC-8 has been prepared jointly with the African Development Bank's budget support
operation. AfDB's PASRP-IV was adopted in April 2008, in support of the Government's PRSP
for 2008-2010. The specific areas for IDA support have been identified in close coordination
with other development partners, in aiming for synergy with their respective interventions, and
building on IDA'S comparative advantage in these areas.         These donors will continue to
strengthen their partnership by promoting, through the General Budget Support Framework
(CGAB) process, best practices, better communication, information sharing and coordination,
Government ownership, and improving predictabilityof mid-term expenditures (by 2012) as well
as short-term disbursements.

42.     IMF and IDA staffs have coordinated closely on the identification of the respective
institutions' areas of focus. The IMF Board discussed the second Government's semiannual
performance review on June 30, 2008. The third IMF review, scheduled in September 2008, is
expected to be presented to the Board in December 2008. IMF and IDA staffs are assisting the
authoritiesin the implementation of the country's PRS, providing them with technical assistance
as regards resource mobilization and budget management.

43.      Twenty seven donors currently support the implementation
of the Government's PRS through various modes of assistance:
general budget support (GBS), sector programs, investments
projects, capacity building projects and technical assistance.
Investment projects account for two thirds of external aid, while
budget support accounts for approximately one third of total donor
support.    Provision of budget support in a coordinated and
cohesive way, in line with the Government's overall development
strategy, is evolving in an encouraging way (see box 3). Deeper
coordination of budget support was initiated following the adoption of the first Poverty
Reduction Strategy which encouraged harmonization of aid and the use of country systems.
Currently, nine donors (the ~ u r o ~ Union, the Netherlands, the African ~evelo~mentBank,
                                        e h
Sweden, France, Denmark, Switzerland, Germany and IDA) use the joint CGAB on the basis of
a Memorandum of Understanding (MoU) signed in 2005. Donors are progressively moving to a
joint monitoring and evaluation matrix, evaluating whether disbursement criteria are met. The
synchronization of each institution's project cycle around the Government's budget cycle
remains a challenge, however, making it difficult to completely harmonize program triggers and
the joint monitoring matrix. The respect of the 2008 CGAB review calendar deadlines and the
content quality o f those reviews, which are correlated, raise further predictability-related
challenges.   he effort of the CGAB members is now focusing on further simplification of the
process-toreduce the number of meetings and improve the policy content, as opposed to process,

of the formal exchanges. This is particularly important for the mid-term review in the fall, which
conditions the ability of development partners to agree sufficiently early on a credible program
for disbursement in the first half of the calendar year. For IDA, better field representation and
involvement in the sectoral and technical commissions reviewing the PRSP will improve
coordination with donors beyond joint mission schedules. Last, the coordination of technical
assistancein the field of capacity strengtheningis one of Government's major preoccupations.

                                  Box 3: Donor Coordination/Harmonization

    Burkina Faso was a pilot country for the initiative on the Test of New Forms of Conditionality, implemented
    from 1997 to 2000, under the stewardship of the European Union. In contrast with old practices where
    conditionality was driven by donors, the new initiative tried to strengthen recipient country ownership and
    responsibility for policies (design, implementation and monitoring-evaluation). A major lesson from this
    initiative was that budget support provided through this new form contributed more to improve ownership,
    accountability and the use of country systems and result-based management principles.

    Owing to low administrative capacity and divergent and multiple practices on the side of the donors, transaction
    costs related to the management of budget support appeared excessive for the Government. The need for
    coordination and harmonization was quickly felt. Donors initially coordinated under the leadership of the
    Delegation of the European Union. The first attempt to harmonize donor activities dates back to 2002 with the
    signature of an agreement (SBC-CSLP) between the European Union, Denmark, France, Germany, the
    Netherlands, Sweden and Switzerland with Government approval. IDA and the AfDB became active members
    of the initiative in a later phase, while the IMF has an observer status and can participate on a voluntary basis. In
    February 2004, the Government proposed a revised and more inclusive version, bringing all budget support
    partners into the Cadre Gknkral d'organisation des Appuis Budgktaires (CGAB, an organizational framework
    for coordinated budget support). Focused on (i) ownership; (ii) alignment; (iii) predictability; (iv) and result-
    based approach, the CGAB is built around ajoint performance matrix (Annexes 9-13) which is the principal tool
    for coordination and evaluation.

    The CGAB monitors and evaluates permanently but has three mandatory sessions each year, with a formal
    performance session held at the time of the annual review of the PRSP progress report. All these sessions are
    chaired by the Minister of Economy and Finance, supported by a secretariat made up of a representative of the
    administration and two representatives of the donors. Joint missions are planned to reduce administrative
    transaction costs.

    On the basis of three-year experience, the preliminary conclusion is that the CGAB requires further work to
    rationalize the joint policy matrix, which should become more focused on strategic milestones of the
    government's policy, while minimizing transaction costs. Future work will, among other, focus on improving the
    PRSP monitoring and evaluation system and aligning the CGAB and PRS processes. In particular, the operation
    of the sectoral and thematic Commissions (CST), which is the anchor of the monitoring-evaluation system of the
    PRSP implementation, needs to be strengthened and reliable data made available to better inform the monitoring
    and performance indicators. As regards donor performance, the principal challenge is still related to the
    predictability of budgetary support. The PEFA report, carried out in January 2007, offers a C+ mark for this
    category of donor performance.

    In conclusion, Burkina Faso experience in budget support coordination and harmonization has considerably
    improved with the introduction of the CGAB, but some significant challenges that go beyond the framework of
    budget support remain to be solved to further strengthen the system.



44.      IDA has completely aligned its PRSC-8 missions with the calendar of the CGAB. This
effort of alignment was appreciated by the Government as well as by the other donors. Several
CGAB members have actively participated in IDA'S missions leading to the proposed program.
IDA also collaborates with several other partners in the context of sectoral programs which are
integrated in the national budget mechanism.

D. COMPLEMENTARITY OTHERBANKACTIVITIES
                           WITH


45.     The CAS identifies the PRSCs as the main instrument for providing program support.
Three other operations are direct complementsto the PRSCs 7-9 series and provide financial
and technical assistance in the areas supported by the proposed PRSC-8: (i) the Administration
Capacity Building project (US$7 million equivalent, FY05) which aims to build capacity in
public administration for policy formulation and implementation, streamlining public
administration, modernizing personnel management, and enhancing the poverty focus of
expenditure; (ii) the Statistical Capacity Building project (US$lO million equivalent, FY04)
which addresses shortcomings in the M&E system by strengthening data collection and
reporting; and (iii) the Decentralized Urban Capacity Building project (US$lO million
equivalent,FY07) which aims to support decentralizationin urban areas.

46.     IDA is also supporting a far-reaching decentralization policy through the second
Community Based Rural Development (CBRD) Project (US$74 million equivalent, FY07) that
builds rural communities' governing and management capacities. The first IDA-supported
Community-based Rural Development Project (US$67 million equivalent, FYOI) has reached
thousands of rural communities, helping finance projects ranging from literacy centers to
improved water management. The program has been instrumental in developing the legal and
regulatory framework for decentralization and local government. Village bodies were entrusted
with the legal mandate of local development. The CBRD covers nearly 40 percent of the
country's 8000 villages.

47.     To improve the effectiveness of aid, IDA and other donors have helped establish sector
wide approaches in basic education, health, HIVIAIDS and water supply. In health, a pooled
funding mechanism channels resources directly to health districts and hospitals on the basis of
indicators that can be tracked over time. The HIVIAIDS Disaster Response Project (US$22
million equivalent, FY02) and the Health Sector Support and AIDS Project (US$63 million
equivalent, FY06 and additional financing in FY08) have contributed a total of US$85 million
equivalent to the sector. IDA, together with development partners, is adopting a sectoral
approach in support of decentralization to assist the Government in addressing the human
resource deployment issue as part of a more comprehensive approach to providing access to
basic health services for the poor. In the education sector, IDA has contributed with the Basic
Education Sector Project (US$51 million equivalent, FY02 and additional financing in FY08)
and the Post Primary Education Project (US$23 million equivalent, FY06) to expanding
children's access to basic education. At the primary education level, IDA projects target girls
and the poor in the provinces with the least coverage.

48.     Responding to rising potable water demand in Ouagadougou,the country's fast-growing
capital, IDA was part of a coordinated 13-donor effort to support the Government's medium
term investment plan. Central to that plan was the construction of the Ziga dam and the
treatment and transport facilities necessary for bringing enough water to the 1million Ouagalais.
IDA'S contribution through the Ouaga Water Supply Project (US$70 million equivalent, FYOI)
financed the secondary and tertiary water network in the city, which will double the number of
household water connections and more than triple the population served through neighborhood
standpipes. The project closed on December 2007. IDA has also invested in drainage and

sanitation infrastructure in Ouagadougou and Bobo-Dioulasso, with positive measurable effects
on disease prevention and economic activity in surrounding areas.

49.     In agriculture, IDA-financed Agricultural Diversification and Market Development
Project (US$66 million equivalent, FY06) is supporting increased productivity in the critical
cotton sector, the development of promising agricultural sectors and supply chains (fruits and
vegetables, cattlebeef, poultry) and is financing expanded irrigation and measures to diversify
farm outputs. IDA has emphasized private-sector led irrigation to allow dry-season production
of high-value export crops such as tomatoes and onions. IDA also supports the manufacture and
maintenance of small scale irrigation equipment, as well as institutional and financial support.
Beneficiaries, including women's groups in the project zone, have been able to double the
surface of irrigated land and increase their household income by an estimated 30 percent. IDA'S
agricultural intensification and marketing program is now scaling up earlier investments in
export infrastructure, such as cold storage rooms at airport. Finally, on the regional level, the
Africa Emergency Locust Project (US$8 million equivalent, FY05) is trying to reduce the
vulnerability of Burkina Faso, as well as other neighboring countries, to present future
infestations of locust in agriculture.

50.     Through the Competitiveness and Enterprise Development Project (US$31 million
equivalent, FY03), IDA has provided technical and financial support in relation with the first
pillar of the PRSC series on competitiveness and export diversification, in particular with the
provision of business development services to strengthen capacity at the firm level and to
promote adoption and adherence to international norms and standards, especially for exporting
firms. In the energy sector, IDA has a two-pronged approach to support the provision of low-
cost energy. First, IDA and other donors have financed urgently-needed power generation
capacity in Burkina, as well as investing in regional electricity interconnections with C6te
dYIvoireand Ghana. The goal has been to halve the cost of electricity. Second, IDA'S Power
Sector Development (US$64 million equivalent, FY05) is providing technical and financial
support to increase rural access to modern energy sources, through expansion of the
electrification grid and investment in alternative technologies.    Also, IDA'S Energy Access
project (US$39 million equivalent, FY08) is offering support to the Government's efforts to
increase energy services expansion to rural, peri-urban, and urban areas.

51.     In the transport sector, IDA is part of a consortium of donors that is assisting the
Government in rehabilitating and maintaining essential transport infrastructure. The support also
involves strengthening existing institutions and adopting sound policy and regulatory measures.
IDA-financed Transport Sector Project (US$92 million equivalent, FY03) has rehabilitated more
than 800 kilometers of rural roads, connecting agricultural production zones to markets. On the
regional level, the West Africa Transport and Transit Facilitation Project (US$6 million
equivalent, FY08) is aiming to improve access to Ghanaian ports by facilitating the efficient
movement of traffic along the Tema-Ouagadougou-Bamako transport corridor.



52.     The design of the proposed operation draws on lessons from a recent
Implementation Completion and Results Report (ICRR) for the PRSC 4-6 series that was
circulated to the Board in February 2008. Specifically, a stronger emphasis has been put on the

growth and employment agenda, beyond cotton, and a new focus put on the decentralization
strategy to more effectively address reduction in inequality and increase opportunities for the
poor. In parallel, a stronger focus on capacity building through accompanying IDA projects,
particularly for line ministries, was offered to the Government to ensure readiness of
implementation and implementability during execution. More realism, based on a broader risk
analysis and risk management, guided the design of reforms in the cotton sector, notably
regarding the privatization of SOFITEX, which takes time given the complex issues arising in
the process.



53.     PRSC-8 continues to benefit from several analytical reports recently completed. A set of
studies were completed in FY06 to assist in identifying priority reform for spurring growth,
diversification and employment. These studies included a labor market study and an investment
climate assessment (ICA). In FY07, a Diagnostic Trade Integration Study, an ESW on
Decentralization and a PEFA were made available. In FY08, an education sector review and a
regional water and sanitation PER helped deepen our understanding of two key sectors for the
decentralization process. Annual Doing Business Reports have been instrumental in the
program's design. Section V.C describes in more details how these pieces of ESW influenced
the design of several components of the proposed developmentpolicy program.

54.     The design of PRSC-8 also benefited from many other sources including a recent report
on the diagnosis of the Independent Audit Institution (Cour des Comptes) and its Action Plan
(European Commission, October 2006), and a report on the sources of growth and the
vulnerability of Burkina Faso's Economy to exogenous shocks (Government - MEDEV,
December 2005).


                               V. THE PROPOSED PRSC-8

55.     The proposed operation is the second in a series of three annual PRSCs (PRSCs 7, 8 and
9) which will support the Government's PRS implementation along with other IDA instruments,
including investment credits. This sectiondescribes the proposed PRSC-8.

A. RATIONALEAND OBJECTIVES

56.     PRSC-8 supports the implementation of Burkina Faso's Poverty Reduction Strategy
adopted in 2000 and updated in 2004. The third series of PRSCs continues to focus on reducing
factor costs (electricity, transport, telecoms) and improving the competitiveness of the cotton
sector, but also supports the Government's efforts to enhance the overall business climate and
exports diversification. PRSC-8 also continues to support Government's measures to further
improve access to basic services through greater decentralization and enhance the efficiency and
accountability of PFM. This responds to requests from the Burkinabe public for improved
quality of public services and more transparency in the use of public resources, as well as from
donors for meeting fiduciary standards for budget support.

Development Objectives

57.      The development objectives of PRSC-8 are to support the acceleration of real growth and
the reduction of the poverty incidence in Burkina Faso by supporting actions to:

     (9      improve the investment climate and promote exports by consolidating the cotton
             sector beyond the current crisis, supporting the Extractive Industries Transparency
             Initiative, and facilitating land titles and construction permit delivery, labor market
             effectivenessand the adoption of a key law on energy regulation.

     (ii)    improve access to basic sewices by operationalizing decentralization in basic
             education,health, water and sanitation and developing municipal investments;and

     (iii)   promote efficiency, transparency and accountability in the use of public
             resourcesthrough enhanced public financialmanagement.

B. RESULT    FRAMEWORK


58.     The proposed PRSC-8 would provide support for the implementation of key economic,
social and institutional reforms aimed at strengtheningthe overall performance of the economy,
address key economic vulnerabilities, and contribute to poverty reduction. Over the period 2008-
2010, the reform program is expected to help the Government advance towards its objective of a
visible reduction in poverty incidence by improving the investment climate and implementingan
export-led growth strategy. It would also support progress towards the MDGs, namely, increase
school enrollment and literacy rates, reduce child and maternal mortality, increase access to safe
water, and strengthenthe sustainability of developmentefforts through greater decentralization.

59.     Government development objectives and outcome indicators for all budget support
operations have been jointly selected by members of the General Budget Support Framework
(CGAB) in Burkina Faso. Indicators for the period 2008-2010 are presented in Annex 11 and
their definition and measurement is spelled out in detail in Annex 13.             In light of the
harmonization process, these indicators aim at outcomes that are broader than those that can be
strictly expected from the policy measures supported by this operation. The Government
development objectives to which this operation will specifically contribute and the outcome
indicators that will be used to assess progress are bolded in the matrix presented in Annexes 9
and 10.

60.     In the process of compiling the CGAB matrix for 2008-2010 some performance and
monitoring indicators proposed for PRSCs 7-9 in the PRSC-7 document have been dropped,
although they remain highly relevant. To monitor outcomes of the PRSC series relative to three
objectives: (i) the acceleration of real growth and reduction in the incidence of poverty; (ii)
increased diversification of exports; and (iii) the medium-term financial viability of cotton
ginning companies, the PRSC series will follow a limited number of additional indicators not
retained by the General Budget Support Framework as indicated in Table 2 and seek their
reintroduction in the 2009-2011 CGAB matrix. Performance and Monitoring Indicators for
PRSCs 7-9 are presented in Annex 2.

61.       This section discusses the Government's reforms and policy areas supported by this third
PRSC series and present the rationale for the selection of prior actions for PRSC-8 and program
triggers for the last operation in the series (PRSC-9). Triggers for an eventual fourth series of
PRSCs will be defined during the preparation of PRSC-9, but key potential areas for further
reforms are mentioned in this section.

I. Improving the Investment Climate and Promoting Exports

62.       The PRSP-I1 targets an average growth rate of 6 percent in the medium term in order to
significantly reduce poverty. Achieving this growth level will require measures to strengthen the
country's competitiveness, diversify its economic base and expand market penetration. Efforts
to improve the contribution of total factor productivity to Burkina's economic growth, including
through measures to shift the country's growth pattern from extensive to intensive agricultural
production, will be particularly critical to ensure a more sustainable growth path. This PRSC
will continue to support critical actions in this area, notably to improve the investment climate
and promote exports.

63.       Burkina Faso's investment climate is widely recognized as difficult but has been
improving, thanks to the Government's business regulations reform program implemented in the

past two years. An investment climate assessment (Burkina Faso ranked 154thout of 155
countries in Doing Business in 2005) identified burdensome regulations, corruption, factor
markets (labor and capital) functioning, and insufficient quality and affordability of
infrastructureservices as key constraints. With the support of the IDA and IFC, Government has
improved business entry, ongoing business operations standards and business exit. Rules
pertaining to property registration and labor market have also been reformed. PRSC-8 and
PRSC-9 continue to support business climate reform and focus on quality and affordability of
infrastructure services, land registration, labor market functioning, the promotion of ICT
modernization and the strengthening and decentralization of administrative services to the
private sector.

64.      Burkina Faso needs to increase and diversify its exports which stagnated at 9 percent
of the GDP over the last ten years. The cotton share in total exports revenues increased from 48
percent to 60 percent over the last decade. Diversification is hampered by: (i) poor infrastructure
and high cost of public services and utilities (transportation, energy, telecommunication and
water); (ii) a weak policy and institutional framework for export promotion; (iii) poor services
for supportingproduction and trade (market information, advisory services and finance); and (iv)
weak producer/professiona1 associations. The prospects for improving exports in Burkina Faso
will depend on: (i) improving the financial viability of the cotton companies (on-farm
productivity, ginning efficiency and product quality); (ii) encouraging diversification to other
agricultural crops; and (iii) increasing foreign direct investment in mining (gold production).

65.      With respect to the cotton sector, three sets of actions are currently being implemented
by sector operators: (1) all three cotton companies have announced their recapitalization plans;
(2) the domestic producer price setting mechanism was revised to more closely align the
producer price with the international cotton price; and (3) efforts continue to improve the
productivity of the cotton sector at both farm and ginning levels. Yields have remained stable at
about 1 ton of seed cotton per hectare. While technical analyses show that Burkina Faso
continuesto benefit from a significant competitiveadvantage in the cotton sub-sector,production
costs, excluding the purchase price from farmers, remain high compared to those in other
countries in the sub-region (for example, variable costs in Burkina Faso are 40 percent higher
than in C8te d71voire,in part reflecting higher electricity costs). In March 2007, the Government
launched a study on the long term sustainabilityof the cotton sector including considerations on
the adoption of transgenic (Bt) cotton. Burkina is the only country in West and Central Africa
that has undertaken field testing of local varieties of Bt cotton. These field trials showed
productivity gains in the range of 19-30 percent and a reduction in pesticide use from an average
of six applications to two. Commercialization of Bt seed has been authorized for the first time
beginning with the 2008109 crop and aims for 15,000ha, with scale-up plans aiming for national
coverage within four years. The Government is also playing a leading role within the sub region
in developingan appropriate biosecurity regulatory framework.

66.     Burkina Faso is about to move from a one-commodity economy (cotton) to a two-
commodity economy with gold exports becoming the second source of export earnings.
However, the Government faces challenges with the proliferation of mining sites. The Ministry
of Mining does not have the capacity to monitor the industrial mining companies, and more
critically, the minerals extracted will have to be traced and taxed effectively to benefit the state
budget without discouraging private investors. More broadly, efforts are ongoing through the

Extractive Industry Transparency Initiative to ensure an optimal contribution of revenue from
mining to benefit developmentand poverty reduction.

Progress since PRSP-7 and focus of PRSC-8

67.     The government has continued its efforts to improve the competitiveness of the
cotton sector: (i) the producer pricing mechanism has been revised to be better aligned with the
international market price and SOFITEX started to reduce its operating costs in 2007, has
adopted and started implementing a restructuring plan aimed at further reducing its operating
costs in 2008 (PRSC-8 trigger); and (ii) independent financial and operational audits of
SOFITEX for 2004-2007 have been launched and the Government has adopted a strategy and a
calendar for enhanced private sector participation in SOFITEX'Sequity (PRSC-8 trigger).

68.     The new price setting mechanism for cotton was adopted by the AICB, and its
application resulted in a floor price of CFAF145 per kg of seed cotton for producers during
the 2007108 campaign. This price setting mechanism aligns domestic prices with world price
trends, with refinements in the mechanism's formula improving on the version first introduced
with the 2006107 campaign. This new mechanism was applied as agreed in April 2007 and
resulted in a farmgate price CFAF20 lower than a year earlier, translating the impact of the Euro
appreciation over the US dollar, despite a slight increase in world prices. As seen from Table 3,
it was also the lowest farmgate price in the region. With the rise in world cotton prices
beginning in mid-2007, a small supplemental payment of CFAFlOlkg above the floor price was
paid to farmers at the start of the current 2008109 campaign. Despite a significant drop in cotton
output (around 35 percent this year), SOFITEX is expected to generate a profit this year and
other cotton companies (Faso Coton and SOCOMA) are expectedto limit their deficit.

  Table 3: Seed Cotton Farmgate Price: Burkina Faso and other West African Producers
                                             (CFAFIkg)
     I Cotton campaign        I CIV    Burkina Faso      Mali          Benin        Senegal




      Source: staff estimates.

69.    As intended, in December 2007 SOFITEX adopted a plan to reduce its operating
costs (Box 4). Costs will be reduced by 3 percent this year, but could reach initial objectivesof a
10percent reduction by the end of the 2008-09 campaign. Restructuring efforts have been partly
offset by: (i) the increase in fixed costs due to a drastically lower output volume (a drop by
around 35 percent); (ii) a continued rise in petroleum prices; and (iii) the late application of this
restructuring plan, while the cotton campaign was already well engaged. Nonetheless, thanks to
this effort and since world prices for cotton began rebounding in mid-2007, SOFITEX is
expected to exhibit a moderate profit at the end of the campaign. AICB (the cotton inter-

  profession association) has much appreciated this effort by SOFITEX and has convinced other
  cotton companies in Burkina Faso to adopt a similar restructuring plan. A continued focus on
  improving SOFITEX's competitiveness is likely to have a lasting impact on the efficiency of the
  whole sector.

  70.       Results from the financial and operational audits of SOFITEX, [both of which have
  been launched], are expected by end-2008. Interested audit companies have responded to a
  bid, from which a short list of 6 companies has been retained. Auditors have been consulted and
  the technical and financial proposals-examined. The selection of the audit company retained is
  expected to be concluded by mid-July 2008. Also, the Government has defined a detailed
  calendar of the audit process, specifyingthe different steps to be undertaken.

   Box 4: SOFITEX's implementation of its cost restructuring plan for the 2007108 campaign

  In a context of cumulated deficits, the government committed to reduce SOFITEX's operating costs by 10 percent to
  attract external participation in the company's equity.

  Yet, the achievement of such a target is challenged by a series of paradigms that are beyond SOFITEX's control:
  SOFITEX is known to have the lowest operating costs among other cotton companies in the CFA zone although Burkina
  Faso's oil products, electricity and road transportation are the highest priced in the WAEMU, and workers' wages are
  higher than in neighboring countries. Additional challenges are posed by incompressible charges, and the decrease in
  cotton seed crops (35 percent), which will trigger a proportionate increase in fixed unitary charges.

  In spite of those external challenges, SOFITEX has identified several areas in which costs can be reduced: (i) fiber
  discharging; (ii) cotton seed transportation; (iii) ginning; (iv) financial costs; (v) administrative costs, (vi) seed production;
  and (vii) rate negotiations. To achieve these goals, controlling bodies will be strengthened, while computer-based
  networks are expected to help scale-up information.

  SOFITEX has provided some impact warnings and observations about some of those measures. Getting back to area (i), it
  is reported that road carriers are reluctant to discharge to the agreed rate, insurance companies and ship-owners are
  maintaining the war tax, and rental agreements for harbor warehouses are to be negotiated on a duration-basis instead of a
  tonnage-basis. As for (ii), it is worth noting that the 2007108 fuel oil price is 10 percent higher than the average 2006107
  price. Measure (iii) is facing weather-related challenges as it is necessary to complete ginning before the beginning of the
  rain season. Measures (iv), (v) and (vi) are currently in progress and benefit from donor participation.

  SOFITEX estimates that internal cost restructuring measures could yield over CFAF4 billion. Given that the 2007108
  ginning yield is lower than the 2006107 season, the prime cost of the fiber will go up by 1.5 percent for the overall costs
 until it reaches the ginning process. On fixed charges, the divisor is expected to decrease by 35 percent due to the decline
  in production. The impact will be substantial in terms of fiberlkg even though it should lead to stabilization (or a drop) in
 terms of value. Last, the margin between seed and oil factory is exvected to shift to positive levels thanks to the rise in the
1 cession                       -
          price as a consequence of the escalating world prices of oleaginous products.
  (Source: SOFITEX's note to its Board, December 6,2007)


  71.       The Council of Ministers adopted in December 2007 a strategy to bring additional
 private investment into SOFITEX's equity. The State will be selling the shares (30 percent of
 the company's equity, i.e. CFAF11.7 billion or 1,169,600 shares) that the Fonds Burkinabe' de
 De'veloppement Economique et Social (FBDES) is holding in portage as substitution for
 DAGRIS's unfulfilled recapitalization share.                        This strategy proposes to identify a unique
 strategic investor that is a known and reputable actor in the sector, with sufficient financial
 capacity and a good business reputation. Transfer of equity would take place before end-2008.
 The Council of Ministers is expected to be informed of the negotiations as they progress. After
 this operation is completed, the State will remain with a minority share of 35 percent in
 SOFITEX's equity (Table 4).
