72.     On EITI, the initiation phase started late, but swift progress has been observed in
the second quarter of 2008. A decree was adopted by the Council of Ministers in April 2008
that promulgates a supervision committee under the Ministry of Economy and Finance for the
EITI. This committee is in charge of overseeing the implementation and supervision of the
Initiative in a participatory manner. The Committee will regularly publish data on revenue from
mining exploitation as well as all payments made by mining companies to the State. At the
Government's request, the Work Bank is providing technical assistance on EITI and is helping
the authorities develop their institutional framework. Stakeholders consultations have been
initiated with the launch of a workshop in June 2008 (a PRSC-8 trigger). The initiationphase of
EITI seeks to reach a broad-based consensus with stakeholders (industrials and civil society) on
the objectives, organization and planning of the process for implementation of the Initiative,
which takes time. Over time, accrued transparency in the use of mining proceeds and enhanced
confidence on the part of foreign investors will contribute to more sustainable growth in the
sector and provide enhanced fiscal returns from mining activities.

73.     With respect to land registration, a perennial legislation for land titling issuancewas
adopted and a one-stop shop for land title delivery was set up in March 2008-(a             PRSC-8
trigger). In addition, many related recommendations suggested by the Doing Business program
have been adopted in the Finance law for 2008: (i) the permission to transfer the property from
the local municipality has been suppressed; (ii) two taxes (fees for acceptance decree publication
after government approval and transfer tax at the Registry) have been merged; (iii) land can be
acquired for business purpose without further investments required, easing credit access for
investors; and (iv) the documentation required to transfer property has been simplified (in
particular, the "attestation de situationfiscale" is no longer required). All these steps reduce the
overall number of procedures and associated delays with land registration.

74.     Following the adoption of a new regulatory law on urban planning and construction in
June 2006, which had then consolidated the existing legislation into a single document, a decree
was adopted by the Council of Ministers in December 2007 formally creating the one stop
shop for building permits deliverance (Centre de Facilitation des Actes de Construire or
CEFAC)-a         PRSC-8 trigger. The CEFAC have been operational as construction licensing
outfits within the Maison de 1'entreprise since May 2008. This facilitates all administrative
processes linked to the construction sector and will contribute to improve Burkina Faso's
business environmentby reducing transactionprocesses, delays and ultimately costs.

75.     Another step in this process has been the set up in January 2006 of the one-stop-shop
center, CEFORE ("Centre de Formalitks des Enterprises") within the "Maison de
I'Entreprise", a private sector association, to assist entrepreneurs in the process of business
creation. The second CEFORE was set up in February 2007 in the second largest town of the
country, Bobo Dioulasso. The concept of one-stop-shop is driven by the notion of efficiency and
convenience for entrepreneurs, who are provided several support services at the same place. In
that instance, entrepreneurs or investors interact and accomplish the procedures with a sole
administration.    A strong partnership has been created between CEFORE and the other
administrations involved in the process. The CEFORE is in charge of requiring approvals from
Greffe Commercialdu Tribunal de Grande Instance for the Register of Commerce,the Direction
Gknbale des Impots for the Existence Declaration and the Fiscal Identification Number, the
National Social Security institution for social security declarations and the Chamber of
Commerce. The immediate gains are time and cost savings for users, both in terms of cost of the
procedures and reduction of informal payments incurred by several administration counterparts.
Revenue collections for fiscal and social security administration are improved by having more
formal enterprises with fiscal identification number and social security registrations. The setting
up of the two CEFOREs has facilitated the creation of 2,581 formal enterprises in 2006 and
2,735 new enterprises in 2007. In addition, both procedures and time to start a business were
reduced from eight to six and from 34 days to 18 days, respectively. Also, some associated costs
have been cut down, which translated in a cost reduction of business creation from 120.8percent
to 82.1 percent of per capita gross national income (GNI). These reforms have been reflected in
the 2008 Doing Business report, in which Burkina was ranked 105thout of 178countries for the
business start up indicator, which is a major improvement from a year earlier (135th out of 175).
Also, the improvements qualify Burkina Faso to rank among the 5 best reformers in Africa as
assessed by the IFC for that year.

76.     With respect to labor market functioning, the PRSC-8 trigger was met with the signing
of three labor code implementation texts enhancing working time flexibility and lifting the
limitation on fixed-term contract renewal in certain sectors, including mining, with approval
from the labor associations. A new labor code incorporating these changes was approved by
Parliament in 2008. Over time, this reform should encourage a shift of employment from the
informal toward the formal sector.

77.     On electricity regulation, in November 2007 the Parliament revised the law on
electricity generation, transmission and distribution (a PRSC-8 trigger). Key benefits from this
new legislative framework include : (i) clarification of the mandates of key sector institutions
and a clearer segmentation of the sector between producers, transporters and distributors,
together with the establishment of a regulator responsible to maintain a balance between actors
and facilitate access to information for all (including private sector and civil society); (ii)
adoption of more transparent tariff setting mechanisms; and (iii) the definition of contractual
terms under which SONABEL's activities could be sub-contracted to private sector actors. With
this electricity law now revised, the legislative framework is set for an affermage of SONABEL.

Focus of the planned follow-on operation (PRSC-9)

78.     PRSC-9 would continue to support the cotton sector through measures aimed at
ensuring profitability of the sector, mitigating the impact of the latest crisis and enhancing

transparency and efficiency in ginning operations, in the context of SOFITEX's upcoming
privatization. While maintaining a competitive cotton sector is critical to the country's exports
and job supply, PRSC-9 would also support the development of market infrastructure (ICT
reform) and services to private sector enterprises, which are both critical for export
diversification and job creation. Improving access to information technology by supportingthe
Government's ambitious modernization plan, susceptible to create a comparative advantage at
the sub-regional level and facilitate enterprise procedures, including beyond Ouagadougou and
Bob-Dioulasso, would improve the country's attractiveness to entrepreneurs, both foreign and
domestic.

79.     While the Government is implementing its strategy to reduce state participation in
SOFITEX's equity, the most urgent task for the sector is to continue to improve its
competitiveness through a combination of measures capable of bringing short and medium-term
results. Over a four-to-five year horizon, the Government is aiming for generalized used of Bt
seeds to reduce pesticide use and costs, to raise yields and strengthen competitiveness on world
markets. The World Bank is supporting the Government's vision through a regional biosafety
project as well as through an Agricultural Diversification and Market Development project
(PAFASP), which has a cotton sector support component.

80.     In the shorter term, the Government can leverage its majority stake in SOFITEX to push
for the adoption of further cost-cutting measures and use of modern management techniques. As
discussed earlier, with SOFITEX controlling 85 percent of the cotton market in Burkina Faso, it
would be expected that the competing domestic ginning companies (SOCOMA and Faso Coton)
would respond to reforms carried forward by SOFITEX with their own efficiency measures, in
order to maintain their competitiveness.

81.     PRSC-9 would support a set of complementary measures aimed at enhancing
SOFITEX's competitiveness (a PRSC-9 trigger), which are under the control of its Executive
Board. The measures propose to utilize available instruments to improve the company's cost
structure and the quality of information available to its management. In particular, they focus
on: continuing to cut costs as already approved by SOFITEX's Board (Box 3); producing
certified accounts for SOFITEX's activity in 2008; and contracting a credit rating agency to
provide rating advice for SOFITEX, as an initial step to improve the company's prospects to
raise capital on the market (such as a regional stock exchange, for instance). Finally, the
measures support the government's stance within the AICB to advocate and monitor the strict
implementation of the price mechanism for the 2008109 campaign, as defined by the Smoothing
Fund's governance rules, a necessary step until the rules and practices are mainstreamed by all
actors in the sector.

82.     PRSC-9 would support the further development of the Information Communication
Technology (ICT) sector, particularly through the submissionto Parliament of two critical texts
(a PRSC-9 trigger): a law pertaining to digital signature and a decree precising the process for
the certification of digital signature, both of which are necessary conditions for a take off in e-
commerce and the launch of e-Government. Burkina Faso has recently launched an ambitious
program to accelerate ICT sector growth. The Government has adopted an ICT Promotion
Policy and a sectoral strategy document. New legislation for Electronic Communications
Services highlighting the convergence of the Telecom, Broadcast and Information Technology

has been prepared and is being reviewed by the Government for its adoption. Some feasibility
studies are underway to accelerate broadband penetration and access in rural and remote areas.
Finally, the Government is adapting its legislationto facilitate e-transaction and the roll-out of e-
Government applications.

83.    As in other countries, e-Government in Burkina Faso could contribute to: (i) a more
efficient use of limited institutional and human resource capacity; (ii) a reduction in the unit cost
of public service delivery (lower transactions costs); (iii) increased tax collection; (iv) the
maintenance of a more robust control framework in public financial management; (v) the
reduction in the cost of doing business; and (vi) overall progress in public sector efficiency,
transparency and accountability.

84.    PRSC-9 support would focus on simplifyingadministrative transactions for business
creation and management beyond Ouagadougou and Bobo Dioulasso, to facilitate formal
job creation in other cities.          Two CEFOREs are currently operational, the first in
Ouagadougou, in the Center region, and the second in Bobo Dioulasso, in the South West area.
Both places account for almost 60 percent of private sector enterprises in Burkina Faso. In order
to better develop rural enterprises, the government included in its business environment reform
program the deconcentration and rationalization of private sector support services in other areas
of the country, through the extension of the CEFORE in the Northern and Central-Eastern
regions of the country (a PRSC-9 trigger), scheduled for FY09. The package offered by
CEFORE not only reduces procedures for business creation but also contains a set of primary
capacity building advices and workshops for new entrepreneurs to strengthen their skills. They
will be located within the local antennas of the Maison de lJEntreprisedu Burkina Faso, which
already offers counseling services and matching grant funds for consultancy services and training
for business under the IDA-funded project Competitive and Enterprise Development Project.

11. Improving access to basic services through deepened decentralization

85.    Burkina Faso is currently fully engaged in the operationalization phase of its
decentralization reform, following a process which started with the first municipal elections in
1995 and continued for a decade until the adoption in 2004 of the basic law fixing the new
decentralization architecture (Code Ge'niral des Collectivitis Territoriales au Burkina Faso).
The Code Ge'niral clarified key strategic directions, created two levels of decentralization(351
municipalities, of which 302 rural and 49 urban, and 13 regions), and delegated in principle to
local authority (LA) eleven areas of competencies covering essential basic services (including
basic education, water and sanitation, health and the environment) as well as the corresponding
human and financial resources. This reform led to the organization of local elections in 2006,
from which the current political and administrative personnel at the local government level is
issued. The latest phase in this decentralizationprocess aims at adopting a series of application
texts defining with precision the new responsibilities of local authority and the corresponding
modalities for effective transfers of resources from the center.

86.    Practical implementation of the Code has been envisaged on a pilot basis, but visible
progress has been slow.         Effectively operationalizing decentralization requires first that
stakeholders' concerns regarding the transfer from the center of administrative, financial and
human assets are addressed appropriately, and that the quality of basic services delivered locally

is improved. Given the difficulty to solve all problems at once, the Government is targeting the
current wave of reforms toward the operationalizationof specifically designated ptiority sectors
and the definition of mechanisms for effective intergovernmentalfinancial transfers. Priority has
been given to the transfer of delegated powers and the corresponding human and financial
resources to a single type of local authority,that of urban councils, and while significantprogress
has been made in the design of draft provisions, the adoption of definitive texts and the effective
transfer of resources remain a challenge stalling the process.

87.     At the administrative level, transfers to priority sectors require that modalities in
delegation of power and conditions for assistance to local governments by the central
administrationsand branch offices be clarified. The Code Ge'niral has only defined general
principles and rules for the transfer of financial resources to local governments for the
administrationof structures they became responsible for, detailing rules for contracting,assisting
and reporting between different layers of administration. The application of those general rules
at a sector specific or administration specific level requires precisions and clarifications on
missions, functions, terms of reference of each administrative unit and practical modalities.
More specifically, the administrative transfer procedure comprises three levels of validation: (i)
the decree transferring powers and resources, that materializes the administrative implementation
of the law, (ii) the joint bylaws transferring State property (arrzte's de de'volution), following a
physical and qualitative inventory and stock taking of the infrastructure concerned, and (iii) the
operation arrangements ("protocoles d'op&ration") specifying the duties of the various players
and the administrative levels concerned. "Protocoles" define in much greater details than
"arrEt&sde devolution" under which structuresthe transferred services will be managed and map
functions and responsibilities for all decentralized services. The principle adopted for human
resources is that State personnel is allowed to continue to enjoy their status of State employee,
even if transferred to the service of municipalities,while municipalities can recruit new staff on a
local status.

88.     The Code, pursuant to the principle of progressiveness, has adopted the transfer to
urban municipalities of six of the eleven areas of power identified, namely preschool education,
basic education, health, culture, sports, and youth affairs and leisure. The adoption of the May
15, 2006 decree has validated the first level in the transfer of six areas of power (priority sectors)
to urban councils. The water and sanitation sector has since replaced that of health, which will
from now on enjoy a one year government granted respite to enable it to conduct a survey of the
terms and conditions for a transfer that ensures the preservation of public service quality. In like
manner, the inventory of State property and the breakdown of roles and duties have actually been
drawn up for the education (and health) sectors in at least all the urban municipalities, and in all
the urban and rural municipalities for water and health. What are pending in these sectors are the
practical modalities and the designation of those to sign the operation arrangements between the
government and the municipalities.

89.     At the financial level, a sustainable transfer formula allowing local administrations to
perform in relation with their new responsibilities, coupled with sound financial management at
the decentralized level, is needed. The efficient exercise of the powers transferred depends, to a
large extent, on the effective transfer of financial resources commensurate with the duties and
expenses of the infrastructure of the sector concerned. For instance, in the basic education sector,
what is required in principle, on the one hand, are recurrent expenses for schools, maintenance

and rehabilitation and, on the other, investment resources. Given the paucity of resources raised
by local governments, local development in Burkina Faso will depend essentially on the volume
of intergovernmental transfers, at the exception of ~ u a ~ a d o u ~and ~    o u 'Bobo-Dioulasso, which
each raises annual revenue above US$400 million on average.

90.      Intergovernmental transfers currently include generic grants (dotations ge'nkrales),
fiscal transfers and the Standing Fund for the Development of Local Authorities (Fonds
permanent de de'veloppement des collectivites territoriales or FPDCT). Grants are split into
operation grants (dotation ge'nkrale aufonctionnement or DGF) and investment grants (dotation
ge'ne'rale h l'e'quipement or DGE).         DGF is of general unconditional use for the local
governments' operating budget. It is calculated on a lump sum for each municipality and a
complement commensurate with the population. DGE is of general use for local government's
investment budget. It is based on the population size. DGF and DGE were established for the
first time in 2007 and inscribed in the 2007 budget for an amount of, respectively, CFAF3.3
billion for DGF and CFAF4.2 billion for DGE for all 351 communes and 13 regions. One third
of this amount, CFAF2.2 billion, is financed by the communalpart of the petroleum tax (TUPP),
which goes to the national budget.          In the 2008 budget, DGF and DGE were allocated,
respectively,at CFAF5.1 billion and CFAFI.5 billion. The Code has also made provision for the
setting up of a structure for the funding of investments by local authorities, the FPDCT, and a
US$5 million budget allocation has been earmarked for the institution since 2007, but not yet
spent.

91.      But the relevant areas of authority have as yet not received any resources for the
expenditures specified above, even if consultation and evaluation processes have made it
possible to clarify the following financial transfer procedure. In the field of basic education,
some kind of evaluation of recurrent expenses on the basis of lump sums has been carried out. In
the water and sanitation sector, as the State does not normally cater to recurrent expenses for
duties and the maintenance of water and sanitation infrastructure in non ONEA areas (that is to
say, at the exclusion of urban areas and surrounding rural areas), financial transfers concern
resources for the rehabilitation of water structures, something that is easy to calculate, the more
so as it figures each year in the national budget for the sector.

Progress since PRSP-7 and focus of PRSC-8

92.     PRSC-7, in coordination with other donors, supported the implementation of the Water
and Sanitation National Program and strengthened the Sector Wide Approach endorsed by the
Government in 2006 to provide more equitable and sustainable access to potable water and
sanitation services, especially for the poor. Since then, progress was made to: i) improve the
sustainabilityof water points, in particular by transferring control over 25 water piped systems to
municipalitiesand encouraging management contractsbetween rural communes and professional
operators (of which two were signed by end-June 2008, for the municipalities of Tikare and
Kindi); and ii) continuing support for the implementation of ONEA's hygiene and sanitation
promotion campaign in four secondary cities (Fada N'Gourma, Ouahigouya, Banfora and
Koudougou) as developed in the sanitation strategic plan (both reforms constitute a PRSC-8


19Between 2001 and 2004, Ouagadougou's internal revenue accounted for over 50 percent of all revenue raised by
urban municipalities.

trigger). ONEA, the national water supply and sanitation utility, is considered as one of the best
performers in Sub-SaharanAfrica and benefits from strong programmatic donor support.

93.     With respect to budget deconcentration, PRSC-8 supports the pilot deployment in a
few regions of the main directorates of the Ministry of Finance and Budget involved in the
decentralization,namely, the head office for budget (Direction Ge'ne'rale du Budget, DGB), the
head office for ex ante control (Direction Centrale Du ContrGle Financier, DCCF), the head
office of public accounting (Direction Ge'ne'rale du Trksor et de la Comptabilite' Publique,
DGTCP) and the tax head office (Direction Ge'nkrale des ImpGts, DGI) (PRSC-8 trigger). All
have started implementing an action plan for their branch offices to help the municipalities
prepare and execute their budgets. The operation has also supported the introduction in 20 urban
townships of the accounting module of CICL (Comptabilite'Inte'grke des Collectivite's Locales)
software specificallydesigned to facilitate the production of financial accounts by municipalities,
which the Treasury (DGTCP) has been implementing since 2003, with the support of
Switzerland.    The Government has also adopted a calendar for the development and the
deployment of CICL's administrative management module. These actions constitute a PRSC-8
trigger that aimed at improving financial management capacity at the local level, a prerequisite to
mitigate risks that decentralized resources are ill managed.

94.     In the implementation of decentralized basic services, the central Government, under
the direction of the Decentralization and Local Administration Ministry, has started a process of
clarification of the rules leading to the organization of an interministerial workshop for the
adoption by the Council of Ministers of a decree transferring competencies to local governments,
the adoption of arrite's de de'volutionin two of the three priority sectors (education and water and
sanitation)and the dissemination of the new rules adopted (two PRSC-8 triggers, one per sector
concerned). In one sector originally targeted - health     -  while the real estate assets of health
facilities were transferred to 49 urban municipalities, a one year moratorium was required to
better understand the implication for staff and medical equipment, while the Ministry's HR
Directorate developed a health personnel hiring and training plan foreseeing the hiring of over
5,000 persons by 2010, and the training of some 540 health specialists; also, a human resource
management software has been operational as part of the implementation of the HR development
plan. The signature of the arrite's de de'volution in the health sector constitutes another PRSC-8
trigger. In education, the extension of the school funds model to 22 provinces is progressing with
the adoption of a credit delegation. However, full transfer of competencies is contingent upon
the implementation of operation protocols. Ultimately, the new decentralized system is expected
to address more effectively the low level of teaching hours and the high unit costs in the building
of schools. While progress in decentralizing basic services is more limited than originally
anticipated by the program, particularly in that no effective transfer of competencies have
actually taken place in any of the targeted sectors, the completion of administrative steps
described above constitute real progress deserving continued support from the World Bank and
the donor community for the implementation of decentralizationreforms in Burkina Faso.

95.     All these steps taken, however, and the delays incurred in this process, have clarified
the severe structural constraints due to the country's historic context and vested interests that
slows central administration decision making. Instead of being seen as an opportunity to
entrench democracy at the local level and scale up the provision of proximity services, the
decentralization project is perceived as bearing significant risks, particularly with regard to the

quality of basic public services, since local authorities are not being adequately trained to take
over the newly transferred duties. Moreover, fears by the central government and urban elites of
losing power in the context of a pyramidal and centralized administration where the outlying
districts are under their strict and constant control, fears by State civil servants of losing job
security or benefits, and fears of seeing their living standards drop in the event of their physical
move from large urban areas to small towns or rural areas, constitute resistances which will
continueto weigh on the implementationof decentralizationreforms in the country.

96.     While pursing the process of implementing the Decentralization Code, the Government
has prepared a blueprint Strategy Paper on Decentralization (CSMOD), with a more
detailed action plan for 2008-2010 which will help address the above constraints. The
CSMOD defines guidelines and core axes of implementation, including (i) social appropriation
of decentralization; (ii) local capacity building (support for the design and implementation of
local development plans); (iii) steering and coordination of the decentralization process; and (iv)
transfer of powers and resources. This last axis, in particular, addresses specificallythe transfer
of powers provided for by law, the question of human resources related to the powers
transferred, budgetary resources required, and monitoring-and evaluation aspects of the reform
process.

Focus of the planned PRSC-9

97.     The government plans to advance the effective exercise of power by municipalities on
a pilot basis in two sectors where administrativedecentralization has most progressed: in basic
education and in water and sanitation. These two sectors were chosen for their direct impact
on poverty alleviation, and their high intensity in both infrastructure management and social
processes, with high learning potential for decentralization actors and the state. The planned
measures will serve as an opportunity to test the solidity of the institutional scaffolding erected,
and the true capacity of the actors to play their role in the efficient management of local affairs.

98.     This program should have a substantive impact on fiscal flows and eventually, on
poverty reduction. For basic education, for example, it will, in the long run, allow for the
placement at the disposal of municipalities of all the teaching staff needed. Their number,
estimated at 32,760 during the 2006/2007fiscal year, represents half the total strength of State
civil servants. This will also entail the transfer of recurrent expenses for the management of
8,182 primary schools, and investment resources for the building of 3,500 schools per annum on
average. Recent surveys conducted in Burkina Faso have confirmed the link that exists between
the level of poverty and that of providing basic social services. In the field of education, for
example, whereas poverty affects 44.6 percent of heads of households who have never gone to
school, it only affects 16.7 percent of those who have completed primary school. The expected
improvement in the quality of services provided by local authorities will contribute to the
enhancement of the human development index, and, consequently, to a substantial reduction in
poverty levels.

99.     The proposed approach in basic education is to target the transfer of resources for
recurrent costs to at least 49 urban municipalities and investment resources to 13 regional
capitals on the basis of experience drawn from the management of urban municipal schools
(PRSC-9 trigger). While the Ministry of Basic Education and Literacy (MEBA) is in charge of

the sector, the duties and responsibilities are shared by the central services of the Ministry, the
decentralized provincial departments, the structure charged with the implementation of the basic
education sectoral development policy and the Basic Education Ten-Year Development Program
(PDDDEB), responsible for building new infrastructure. The outcome of the experience will be
used as a basis for extendingthe same services to all other municipalities in the country.

100.   In water and sanitation, pursuant to the application of the progressiveness principle,
PRSC-9 targets the signature of operational protocols for the management of the services
transferred to the territorial collectivities in at least one hundred rural municipalities
(PRSC-9 trigger). The General Directorate of Water Resources (DGRE) is mainly responsible
for services in rural areas through an operational management process that is jointly carried out
by the central administration, its deconcentrated services and municipalities. In the urban and
semi-urban areas, the National Water and Sanitation Board (ONEA) is in charge of the sector.
The Board will continue to exercise its control authority over the sector's infrastructure and
equipment. As in the basic education sector, the outcome of the lessons drawn from this
experiencewill serve as a basis for the extension of the serviceto all the other municipalities.

101.   In the health sector, it is expected that during the one year respite mentioned above the
Ministry will conduct studies on the best terms and conditions possible for the transfer of health
servicesto local authorities so that, when implemented,it will not jeopardize service quality. The
main reservation made in this regard concerns the risks involved in dismantling the management
autonomy of State district hospitals, in particular the immediate transfer of the powers of the
management team made up of civil servants (notably medical doctors) to local authorities (urban
municipalities and regions) whose capacities are deemed as yet inadequate to efficiently run
health centers.

102.   The Standing Fund for the Development of Local Authorities (FPDCT) is an
expression of the political will of the Burkinabe government to make provision for the
financing of local government investments through its own budgetary allocations and
funding from donors. It is designed to serve as a sole and unifying financial framework, based
on the national system, and for the harmonization of both government operations and those of
technical and financial partners, in keeping with the Paris declaration on development aid
effectiveness. On the basis of a reciprocal commitment, materialized by a municipal agreement,
the State will, through the Fund, be placing at the disposal of local authorities' investment
resources and the relevant technical support. For instance, in the water and sanitation sector,this
fund will enable the implementation of local development plans (Plan Communaux de
Dkveloppernent en AEPA/PCD-AEPA). The local authorities have in turn committed to carry out
investmentson the basis of preset performance levels. The Fund should, by so doing, be opening
up competition between local authorities for the acquisition of the resources placed at their
disposal.

103.   The PRSC-9 would support the operationalization of the Fund on the basis of a pilot
phase wherein beneficiaries will be limited to urban councils so as to test the facility and the
operation strategies adopted. The PRSC-9 trigger targets the provision of investment resources
to the Fund for 2008 and 2009. This phase will entail the strengthening of the planning
capacities of the beneficiary towns in order to make them ready for the acquisition of resources
from the Fund.      The measure will also receive support through the IDA-funded Urban

Development Support Project, known as Regional Development Poles (RDP), which will
facilitate absorptionof these funds at the municipal level.

111. Promoting efficiency, transparency and accountability in the use of public resources
through enhanced public financial management

104.    Burkina Faso has been implementing an ambitious reform agenda since the adoption in
2002 of a consolidated action plan to improve its budget management system (Plan d'Action
pour le Renforcement de la Gestion Budge'taire, PRGB). Achievements to date include: (i)
stronger budget legislation which reflects most of the WAEMU directives on public finance; (ii)
improved alignment of budget allocations with PRSP priorities with the introduction of a global
MTEF; (iii) enhanced budget execution with the operation of a computerized expenditure
management system that captures all domestically financed expenditures; and (iv) improved
budget reporting and auditing with the introductionof an independent Audit Court.

105.    The 2007 PEFA report acknowledged these achievementsbut also highlighted remaining
weaknesses. Specifically, it identified scope for improvement in the following areas: (i) level of
mobilization of domestic revenue and efficiency of tax administration; (ii) public availability of
budget information; (iii) internal controls over salary and non salary expenditures, including
compliance with procurement regulations; (iv) effectivenessof the internal audit process and (v);
effectivenessof the external audit and parliamentaryoversight process.

106.    In February 2007, the authorities approved a public financial management strategy
(Strate'gie de renforcement des finances publiques, SRFP) for 2007 to 2015. The three-year
rolling action plan that was prepared to implement this strategy (Plan d'Actions Sectoriel
Triennal, PAST) focuses on four priorities for 2007-2009: (i) generating more domestic revenue;
(ii) reforming public procurement; (iii) ensuring that expenditures are properly classified so that
poverty-reducing and foreign-financed expenditures can be tracked; and (iv) reinforcing audit
systems. In November 2007, the Parliament created the ASCE, which was given the
competencies of the three entities involved in the government's anti-corruption activities. This
represents an opportunity to scale up the fight against corruption and introduce greater clarity in
the respective roles of the various internal oversight bodies.

Progress since PRSP-7 and focus of PRSC-8

107.    Administrative structures in charge of developing program budgets were strengthened
in the ministries of infrastructure and environment; and baseline data were collected to
improve predictability in budget allocation (including for road maintenance), enhance
effectiveness in public spending, and strengthen these ministries' institutional capacities (PRSC-
8 trigger). Program budgets were formally prepared in both ministries, yet their quality needs
strengthening. Budget allocations for the two ministries were marginally increased in 2008
compared to 2007. Staff in budget planning functions benefitted from training. The Ministry of
Environment is expected to finalize shortly its sectoral policy document.

108.    On public procurement, PRSC-8 has sustained the open market reform.
Procurement units have been established since April 2008 in two regions: the Hauts-Bassins
(Bobo Dioulasso) and the Center East Region (Tenkodogo); and in two line ministries: the

Ministry of Basic Education and Literacy and the Ministry of Health. Despite delays in the
deployment of the Integrated Information System on Open Markets (SIMP), baseline data
(reference indicators) about the six most important ministries (in terms of open market) has been
collected (on a manual basis) for the last six months of 2007. This data served as benchmark to
set-up the monitoring and evaluation system, using the performance indicators recommended by
the OECD. Conclusions have been drawn on the basis of the available data and are expected to
be published in a report by end-2008.

109.    PRSC-8 supports efforts to enhance the independence and efficiency of the Audit
Court by protecting its budget from in-year freezes starting from January 2008. Specifically,the
Government adopted a decree on administrative liquidation and inscribed under title 4 the budget
of the Court (a PRSC-8 trigger). An IDF grant and an-EU funded technical assistance project
have lowered the financial and technical constraints on the Court's operations and are addressing
organizationalissues.

Focus of the planned PRSC-9

110.    PRSC-9 would support two of the four priorities contained in the authorities' action
plan, where the impact of the Bank's support is likely to be strongest: (i) improving
expenditure classification and transparency in the use of public expenditure; and (ii)
strengthening the internal and external oversight systems. This focus addresses three of the
five main weaknesses identified by the 2007 PEFA and leverages the opportunity created by the
creation of the ASCE. The two other priorities of the authorities are being addressed separately.
Procurement reform is one of the two focus areas included in the AfDB budget support operation
(PASRP-4). This operation was prepared as part of a joint identification mission with the Bank.
The other priority, which deals with the resource mobilization agenda, could not be included in
the current operation as the authorities are engaged in a wide-ranging policy dialogue with the
IMF on tax policy, which may significantlyaffect the revenue side of the budget cycle.

111.    The implementation of a functional classification of budget expenditure is an
objective within reach for Burkina Faso that would improve the link between policy and budget
allocationand would also contribute to increasetransparency. Although the government financial
management information system supports the use of functional classifiers, the parameters
included in the system do not yet yield meaningful data. A September 2007 AFRITAC mission
has made some recommendations to solve outstanding technical issues and one of PRSC-9
triggers would support the full implementation of these measures, including the publication of
information of pro-poor expenditure. Once they have been produced, functional expendituredata
would be made available on a budget information website that would also include other essential
PFM documents on budget execution and financial transfers to local authorities. This would
significantly contribute to improved transparency and would have a positive impact on the
country's PEFA assessments.

112.   The creation of the ASCE provides an opportunity to step-up the authorities' efforts in
the fight against corruption and to address long-standing issues that have been hindering the
effectiveness of the internal audit system. The 2005 CPAR had highlighted the fact that, while
the various internal audit bodies (IGE, now replaced by ASCE, IGF, and ministerial
inspectorates) had partially overlapping mandates, none had sufficient resources to fully exercise

them. They were used on an ad hoc basis, usually when there was suspicion of malfeasance and
there was no adequate follow-up of their conclusions. The 2007 PEFA assessment also showed
that, despite repeated training, these bodies (in particular IGE and IGF) were not fully compliant
with international standards on audit and investigations. In the case of IGF in particular, the lack
of effectiveness of training is explained by excessive turn-over, due to insufficiently attractive
working conditions.

113.     PRSC-9 would support efforts to address these weaknesses by clarifying the respective
roles of ASCE and ministerial internal audit bodies. ASCE would concentrate on the
investigation of corruption cases, focusing on the identification of individual responsibilities
when funds are misappropriated20.Ministerial inspections such as IGF would audit systems and
procedures, assess risks, identify the root causes of irregularities or inefficiencies and make
recommendationsfor improvement in the management of public administrations. PRSC 9 would
also support the production by ASCE of a baseline report on the extent of corruption in the
country and the adoption of a statistical reporting system on pending and closed corruption cases
(PRSC-9 trigger). It will also help review the terms and conditions of employment of
inspectorate bodies' staff to increase staff retention and the impact of training and thus increase
the sustainabilityof capacity development efforts (PRSC-9trigger).

114.     The creation of the independent Audit Court is a major achievement of previous PRSCs.
This achievement needs to be consolidated, as the activity of the Court has slowed down and
delays started to appear in the production of its annual reports in 2006 and 2007. An in-depth
review of the organization of the Court, showed that three main internal factors were to blame:
insufficient availability of human and financial resources; terms and conditions for staff that did
not adequately protect the independence of staff in place (13 out of 16 sittingjudges are on a 5
year term that is renewable only once and would be expected to return to the Ministry of Finance
and Budget after the end of their term) or allow the Court to recruit qualified graduates; and poor
internal organization of the Court's activities. External factors, such as the timely availability of
the government's financial statements, also played a role in the delayed production. A PRSC-9
trigger focuses on the production and publication of the Court's annual reports for 2006 and
2007. This will increase accountability by ensuring the regular publication of the Court's
essential observationson the quality of public financial management.

115.     Key PFM reforms that could be supported by future PRSCs include: (i) methodological
support to the authorities' objectives to adopt a program budget by 2012 in order to further
strengthen the link between policy and budget allocation; (ii) support to the overhaul of the tax
system and tax administration to increase domestic resource mobilization and tax collection
efficiency in a manner conducive to economic growth and private sector development; (iii)
expenditure chain review to increase its efficiency and effectiveness; (iv) support to public
financial management measures needed to accompany the decentralization processes; and (v)
further support to the audit bodies to progressivelyassist them in meeting internationalstandards.




20ASCE will also add value, as per its mandate, by receiving all internal audit reports and drawing conclusions on
risks and trends from their analysis.

D. PRIORACTIONS       FOR THE PROPOSED PRSC-8


116.   PRSC-8 would support selected reforms in the Government's program and in its Letter of
Development Policy (LDP) (Annex 1)that are deemed essentialto its successful implementation.
The rationale for their selection has been discussed in the previous section. This list is fully
aligned with the joint performance matrix of the budget support group (Annex 9 - General
Budget Support Framework (CGAB-CSLP) - Performance and Disbursement Criteria Matrix:
2007-2009). Compliance with these prior actions as defined in the English version of the
Financial Agreement forms the basis for IDA to proceed with the proposed operation.

117.   Prior actions for PRSC-8, all of which were derived from triggers identified during the
preparation of PRSC-7, were all met by mid-July 2008. As detailed in Table 5, four out of the
thirteentriggers identified in PRSC-7 were adjusted to account for exogenous shocks (first action
on SOFITEX's operating costs), slight delays in implementation due to organizational capacity
weaknesses (action 4 on EITI), and the necessity to adopt a more realistic approach on
decentralization (action 7 on education and action 9 on public private partnerships for the
management of water piped systems in rural municipalities) due to limited implementation
capacity at the local level and to stakeholders' decision to reform more gradually with regard to
the decentralization of personnel. In some areas (doing business, labor market efficiency), the
Government carried its reform agenda significantly beyond the goals set during the preparation
of PRSC-7; also, the PFM reform agenda has been progressing extremely well over the last few
PRSCs, including since the start of this new series; and the reform of the domestic price setting
mechanism for cotton constitutes a remarkable effort from national stakeholders, which has
allowed the sector to weather the latest crisis, despite severe exogenous shocks. On balance, the
program supported by this PRSC-8 is substantial and demonstrates the Government's strong
commitment to reforms.

    E. PROPOSEDTRIGGERS PRSC-9         FOR


    119.      PRSC-9 will continue to support the Government's PRS strategic objectives. As per any
    development policy operation, an adequate macroeconomic framework will have to be
    maintained during the program period. This proposed list of triggers is fully aligned with the
    joint performance matrix of the budget support group for 2008 (Annex 10). The detailed
    definition of the measures (policy actions) proposed for 2008 by the CGAB framework is clearly
    spelled out in Annex 12, with a formulation that is fully consistent with the proposed triggers set
    out in Table 6 below.

                                        Table 6: Proposed Triggers for PRSC-9



Pillar I. Improving the investment climate and promoting exports


1.  Cotton sector reforms progress as evidenced by: (i) the strict implementation of the price mechanism for the 2008/09
    campaign, as defined by the Smoothing Fund's governance rules; (ii) the contracting of a credit rating agency to provide
    rating advice for SOFITEX; (iii) the production of certified accounts for SOFITEX's activity in 2008; and (iv) the
    implementation of SOFITEX's restructuring plan to reduce the company's operating costs, as adopted in 2008.


2.  To develop the ICT sector, a new bill pertaining to digital signature has been submitted to Parliament together with a
    draft application decree precising the process for the certification of digital signature..


3.  The supply of services to the private sector has been strengthened and decentralized as evidenced by the opening of
    CEFOREfor the Northern and Central-Eastem regions.


Pillar 11. Improving access to decentralized basic services


4.  Decentralization in basic education is further operationalized as evidenced by: (i) the disbursement on a quarterly basis
    of the financial transfers to 49 urban municipalities inscribed in the 2009 budget law for recurring charges; and (ii) the
    transfer to territorial collectivities and disbursement on a quarterly basis of investment fund resources for classroom
    construction in the 13 urban municipalities that are regional capitals.


5.  Decentralization in water and sanitation is further operationalized as evidenced by the signature of operation protocols
    for the management of the services transferred to the territorial collectivities in at least 100 rural municipalities (in the
    aggregate).

6.  To support economic activities and services in urban municipalities, at least CFAF 5 billion are transferred to the
    Standing Fund for the Development of Local Authorities (FPDCT) in 2008 and CFAF 5 billion are inscribed in the 2009
    budget and disbursed on a quarterly basis.

Pillar 111. Promoting efficiency, transparency and accountability in the use of public resources

7.  The approved budget and information on budget execution (on a functional basis and for each ministry) and on transfers
    to local governments are published on the Government's website.

8.  The annual reports of the Audit Court for 2006 and 2007 have been published.

9.  The High Authority for State Oversight (ASCE) has started its activities as evidenced by the publication of a baseline
    report on the extent of corruption in the country and the adoption of a statistical reporting system on pending and closed
    corruption cases. Terms and conditions of employment of inspectorate bodies' staff across ministries are reviewed to
    make them more attractive.

                           Box 5. Good Practice Principles on Conditionality


Principle 1:Reinforce Ownership

This PRSC is embedded in the General Budget Support Framework (Cadre Ge'nkral d'Organisation des Appuis
Budge'taires - CGAB), an organizational framework for coordinating and harmonizing budget support and its
process between the Government of Burkina Faso and donors engaged in providing budget support. The CGAB in
its current form has been established in February 2005. It focuses on (i) ownership of the reform process; (ii)
alignment with the country's PRSP; (iii) predictability of budget support; and (iv) a result-based approach which
is coordinated and agreed upon with the Government and the donors of budget support.

Principle 2: Agree upfront with the Government and otherfinancialpartners on a coordinated accountability
framework

The group of donors organized in the CGAB support reforms as outlined in Burkina's PRSP and which are
updated and further specified in the annually revised Priority Action Plan (PAP). The PAP combines planned
policy objectives and actions with a medium resource planning framework (MTEF) and a monitoring framework.
Following the PAP'S objectives and monitoring framework, the CGAB donors develop a joint performance matrix
which is the principal tool for coordination and evaluation of budget support. This procedure allows for
accountability along shared objectives and performance indicators.

Principle 3: Customizethe accountabilityframework and modalities of Bank support to country circumstances

Since 2005, IDA'S support through PRSCs is part of the CGAB framework in response to Government demands
to align donor support to lower transaction cost for the Government and to harmonize the policy dialogue along
with the other donors around the PRSP and its priorities, as well as the accountability framework for budget
support. Identification, pre-appraisal and appraisal is completely aligned with the CGAB calendar which offers
three principal sessions with the Government to assess policy outcomes (April, jointly with an assessment of the
Annual Progress Report of the Country's PRSP implementation), to identify policy measures and possible
conditions for budget support (September) and to conduct a midterm review (February). Only reforms truly
owned by the Government, however sensitive, have been retained in the program.

Principle 4: Choose only actions criticalfor achieving results as conditionsfor disbursement

The Bank's policy matrix uses a limited set of conditions (13 in total for PRSC-I), which function as triggers
(prior actions) for disbursement. The chosen policy measures were identifiedjointly with the Government as well
as the other CGAB donors to ensure a critical contribution to the Government's reform program and to coordinate
with conditions emphasized by other CGAB donors. Conditionality is focused only on critical operational actions
which would move the Government's reform agenda further. Nine triggers have been identified for PRSC-9 in
partnership with the Government. The CGAB matrix (Annexes 9-13) provides further program benchmarks,
some of which can be used as conditionality by other donors. The conditionality is also typically coordinated
with the program the Government discusses with the IMF.

Principle 5: Conduct transparent progress reviews conducive to predictable and performance-basedflnancial
support

The CGAB monitors and evaluates permanently progress made with regard to policy implementation and
conducts the above described three mandatory annual review sessions. These three sessions are chaired by the
Minister of Economy and Finance, supported by a secretariat made up of a representative of the administration
and two representatives of the donors. The three sessions are open to interested parties, and the principal results as
well as a summary of the discussions are documented and publicly available. The Government incorporates
feedback from such CGAB meetings into its own reporting, including for the Annual PRSP Progress Report. As
described in Box 3, the current focus is toward enhanced policy content in a review process at times excessively
cumbersome.

                            VI. PRSC-8 IMPLEMENTATION

A. PARTICIPATION      PROCESS


120.    The design of PRSC-8 benefited from broad stakeholders consultations and was
conducted jointly with the preparation of the African Development Bank's budget support
operation. The Government led several meetings in which experts from central and line
Ministries and top officials, including Ministers, consulted local Governments officials, private
sector representatives, and civil society organizations. Among those meetings, one can note:

    the two CGAB-CSLP annual reviews, which represented the last stages of a consultation
    process involving Government experts, private sector and civil society representatives,
    working together in the six established sector and thematic commissions (Commissions
    Sectorielles et The'matiques - CST), allowed candid discussions of the challenges that
    Burkina Faso faces, including on the organization of the cotton sector, the implementation of
    decentralization and the role of independent watchdog institutions for good governance;

    intensive engagement through ESW and M A (whether IDA- or partner-led) to improve
    appropriation of analytic findings by all actors involved. Several workshops took place,
    including on the results of the CPIA exercise, on a regional IDA'S study on cotton, on the
    findings of the Diagnostic Trade Integration Study, on early findings from the Country
    Economic Memorandum on growth and competitiveness, in the conduct of the joint FSAP
    and the joint DSA with the IMF, in the conduct of the Debt Management Performance
    Assessment Tool (DeMPA), on the dissemination of an audit of domestic debt sponsored by
    the Swiss Cooperation, and of an EU diagnosis of the Audit Court.

B. POVERTY     AND SOCIALIMPACTS


121.    The proposed PRSC-8 is expected to have a significant positive direct impact on
poverty reduction. First, the improvement in the investment climate and the promotion of
exports supported by the proposed operation would sustain economic growth. This includes
improving access to and security of land titles in rural areas where most of the poor live and thus
is expected to have a substantial positive impact on access to credit. Second, the strengthening
of public financial management is expected to enhance efficiency, transparency and
accountability in public resource use.       Such measures would also increase the country's
absorption capacity for external resources.       Third, the reforms in public service provision
through decentralization would improve access of the poor to health, education, water and
sanitation services, support their greater participation in the growth process and sustain progress
towards the MDGs. However, as discussed below there is a risk that if not accompanied by the
provision of sufficient resources and strengthened capacity for local Governments to carry out
their new responsibilities,the devolution of basic service delivery may result in reduced quality and
access to these services. To mitigate this, several donors support decentralization efforts in Burkina
Faso, including the ongoing IDA-financed Administration Capacity Building project, the Community
Driven Development project, and the Urban Capacity Building project. In addition, the recently
completed IDA analytical work on decentralization ESW is helping the Government fine tune
the decentralization program, particularly with regard to service delivery for the poor. As part of
its next Country Assistance Strategy, IDA will be considering other instruments beyond PRSCs,

including through budget support, to provide both resources and capacity for local authorities in
their management of social services.

122.    The new price formula adopted by the cotton interprofession could affect poor
households relying on this cash crop as a dominant source of earnings, but this impact can
be mitigated by the smoothing fund, provided that the trend allows a recovery in international
prices over the medium-term. Actually, by the end of the 200712008 campaign, international
cotton prices were at higher levels than the average of the preceding four years, both in dollar
and FCFA terms. Rising international prices result in higher floor prices for cotton producers at
planting season, supplemented post-harvest by a ristourne when prices continue to rise during
the agricultural campaign. In addition, cotton income represents less than 25 percent of the total
income of cotton producers, thus most cotton growers have the possibility to compensate for low
cotton prices by diverting some of their resources to other farm and nonfarm activities (see
Annex 3). In addition to PRSC-8 in support of cotton sector reforms, IDA is also assisting rural
households with several projects, particularly to reduce their risks and increase their revenue. In
particular, IDA is supporting implementation of an agricultural project (PAFASP) to promote
diversification beyond cotton. Also, the adoption of Bt (genetically modified) cotton should
allow a reduction in input factors (reduced use of fertilizersby farmers).



123.    The activities supported by the proposed operation are not likely to have significant
negative environmental impacts. In the cotton sector, PRSC-8 supports Government's efforts
to consolidate the cotton sector beyond the current crisis. Beyond PRSC-8, the Government's
broader strategy in the context of external market factors is anticipated to have a number of
environmental effects which are being tracked. First, the Government has authorized the
commercialization of Bt cotton seeds, expanding from an initial 15,000 ha for the 2008109 crop
year to national scale in four years. Enhancing regulatory capacity to manage environmental
aspects is supported through the IDA-financed West Africa Regional Biosafety Project. Second,
expansion of this seed technology is anticipated to result in a significant reduction in pesticide
use on cotton. Third, land area devoted to cotton production is anticipated to respond to net
income prospects for the crop; these may improve marginally over the PRSC-8 period but are not
expected to return to levels earlier in the decade, and thus although cotton area may expand
beyond levels of the last two years, this will be on already cultivated arable land, and not to the
limits of prior years of peak cotton production. Finally, fertilizer use in aggregate and per
hectare on cotton is not anticipated to increase during the PRSC-8 period. World prices have
increased dramaticallyand are anticipated to remain well above levels of recent years, and while
domestic nominal price increases are for now being moderated by subsidies, the likely medium-
term direction of fertilizerlcotton price ratio is upward, hence a dampening effect on fertilizeruse
for cotton is expected. In the mining sector, PRSC-8 support is for the launch of the EITI, which
will enhance transparency in revenue collected from the sector, without direct consequences on
the environment.

124.    It is important to underscore that Burkina Faso is aware of the importance of ensuring
environmental sustainability and that the Government has tasked the Ministry of Environment to
supervise compliance of pubic policies and programs with environmental regulations. In May
2007, the PRSC environmental team assessed the readiness of the recipient to deal with

environmental issues. The assessment found that Burkina Faso has the necessary regulations
pertaining to environmental assessment but that compliance with such regulations is weak. Like
many other IDA countries, Burkina Faso prepared a National Environment Action Plan (NEAP)
at the beginning of the 1990s. Unlike the other countries, however, the NEAP of Burkina Faso
was not reflected in a coherent investment program, which could have included capacity-
building components. It implied delays in creating the regulatory framework, a lack of
experience and limited capacity in certain areas of environmental management, particularly
environmental assessment. To overcome that, the Ministry of Environment has prepared a
program budget to reflect its institutional and technical capacity needs until 2010, during the
course of which some of its staff benefited from technical training in budgeting techniques.

125.    A directorate for environmental impact assessment (EIA) composed of 20 technical staff
at the Ministry of Environment is tasked with the analysis of ordinary EIA, strategic planning,
evaluation and environmental audits. Most of the staff has a forestry background and is based in
Ouagadougou. Belgium cooperation has supported the preparation of a national action plan for
2008-2015 to build-up national capacity for EIA. The action plan includes the development of
skills and the strengthening of EIA institutional mechanisms. While the Government's current
technical and operational capacity for EIA is weak, the implementation of the above action plan
through a combination of donor support and additional general budget resources would
contributeto bring EIA capacity to satisfactorystandards.



126.    The 2001 CFAA and CPAR, and the 2004 HIPC AAP, were key inputs in the preparation
of the Government's budget management reform program (Programme de Re'forme de la Gestion
Budge'taire, PRGB). The Government has also used the findings of the 2005 CPAR and the
2007 PEFA assessment to update the PRGB. The PRGB is supported by most donors providing
budgetary support, and is a core element of the MOU for budget support. Under PRSC-1 to
PRSC-7, most recommendations of the CFAA and CPAR have been successfully implemented,
including the implementation of annual audit procedures recommended by the CFAA. PRSC-8
builds on the achievements under the previous PRSCs by supporting the further strengthening of
internal and external oversight, and by including additional measures to enhance policy-based
budgeting and budget transparency. In the context of PRSC-8 preparation, the Bank has
reviewed fiduciary arrangements. Overall, the Bank has judged implementation performance of
the PFM reform program to date and Government's commitment to PFM improvements as
satisfactory.

127.    The Central Bank of the West African States (BCEAO) is the common central bank of
the West African countries, including Burkina Faso. In 2005, IMF staff carried out an on-site
safeguards assessment of the BCEAO and found that progress had been made in strengthening
the BCEAO's safeguard framework since 2002 when the last safeguard assessment was
undertaken. The BCEAO now publishes a full set of audited financial statements, and
improvements have been made to move financial reporting closer to International Financial
Reporting Standards (IFRS). IMF staff noted that the BCEAO has improved the explanatory
notes to the financial statementsand further changes are scheduled for the next fiscal year, with a
view toward a gradual alignment with International Accounting Standard (IAS), as adopted
internationally by other central banks. Furthermore, an internal audit charter has been put in

place, mechanisms have been established to improve risk management and risk prevention, and
follow-up on internal and external audit recommendations has been strengthened. The
assessment identified a number of areas where further steps would help solidify the progress
made. The main recommendations relate to: (i) improvingthe external audit process by adopting
a formal rotation policy and further enhancing the transparency of the financial statements by
adopting IFRS in full; and (ii) enhancing the effectiveness of the internal audit function by
further strengthening the reporting to management of the BCEAO. The status report of the
implementation of recommendations, received from the World Bank in March 2007, indicates
that some progress was achieved.

128.   Bank financial management specialists reviewed the flow of funds arrangement between
the Central Bank (BCEAO) and the Treasury (DGTCP), taking into account the findings of an
audit of the flow of budgetary funds between the Central Bank and the Treasury funded by the
multi-donor Budget Support Group. The team concluded that current arrangements are adequate,
with fiduciary risks within acceptable risk tolerance levels. However, the team recommended
that the information systems of these two institutions be linked, so as to minimize delays and
discrepancies in flows reporting, thus eliminating cumbersome reconciliations. The last audit
report on the flows of funds, which was produced in 2005 and assessed the Central Bank's
foreign exchange environment, revealed no major weakness in the system.

E. DISBURSEMENT AUDITING
                     AND


129.   The proposed operation would consist of a single tranche of SDR 61.5 million (US$100
million equivalent, on IDA credit terms) to be available upon effectiveness and disbursed on the
basis of a withdrawal application. The operation would follow IDA'S disbursement procedures
for development policy credits and would be disbursed against satisfactory implemention of the
development policy operation and would not be linked to specific purchases.           Once the
Financing Agreement becomes effective, the proceeds will be deposited by IDA into a
Government account at the Central Bank which forms part of the country's foreign exchange
reserves; as a due diligence measure, IDA will obtain confirmation from the Government that the
amount of the operation has been accounted for in the country's budget management system
(within 30 days of disbursement). If, after being deposited in this account, the proceeds of the
operation are used for ineligible purposes as defined in the Financing Agreement, IDA will
require the Recipient to refund directly to IDA, an amount equal to the amount of said payment,
promptly upon notice from IDA. Amounts refunded to the Bank upon such request shall be
cancelled. No dedicated account is required.



130.   The Ministry of Economy and Finance will be responsible for overall implementation of
the proposed PRSC-8. The Secretary of the Permanent Secretariat for the Monitoring of
Financial Programs and Policies (SP-PPF) in the MEF will lead the Government's technical team
with representatives of the Ministries of Economy and Finance; Health; Labor and Employment,
Social Protection, Basic Education and Literacy; Secondary Education, Higher Education and
Scientific Research; Environment and Living Conditions; Agriculture, Water and Fisheries;
Commerce, Enterprise Development, and Artisanship; Energy and Mines; Decentralization;
Public Administrationand representativesof public enterprises.

131.    IDA'S supervision effort will be aligned with the Government-led monitoring and
evaluation system underpinning the PRSP-11, the Priority Action Plan, and the General Budget
Support Framework (CGAB). The specific benchmarks and outcomes to be monitored under
PRSC-8 (see Annex 2) are extracted from the Government overall development program (Annex
1I), thus providing significant benefits in reducing transaction costs for the Government. They
are included in the joint performance matrix under the CGAB. IDA staff has been working with
the authorities and the other donors under the CGAB to ensure that coordination of the
supervision efforts will increase predictability and aid alignment with the budget cycle. In
assessing the implementation of the PRSP-11, the CGAB will take into account factors outside
Government's control that may alter the underlying assumptions for a successful PRSP
implementation. Such factors include declining terms of trade, weather-related shocks, and
shortfalls in external assistance and regional instability.

G. RISKS AND RISKSMITIGATION

132.    Several risks couldjeopardize the expected outcomes and benefits of this operation. First,
as Burkina Faso continues to be vulnerable to commodity price fluctuations, further increases
in oil and food prices could undermine macroeconomic stability and progress made in
poverty reduction. High oil prices drain international reserves and increase petrochemical,
electricity and transportation costs with adverse impact on the overall economy. Increased world
prices on basic food commodities translate into higher inflation and hurt the poor, particularly in
urban centers. Should the macroeconomic outlook deteriorate, social tensions could affect
political stability, in the run up to the presidential elections in 2010. Also, lower cotton prices on
international markets weaken the country's economic and fiscal performance and negatively
affect cotton producers' incomes. To address these risks, the Government is implementing a
three-pronged approach, including: (i) implementing a cotton price smoothing fund to help
stabilize profits and losses over time, reducing uncertainties on future income for producers in a
given year; (ii) in the medium term, lowering energy costs by connecting with lower-cost
regional producers and opening the electricity and petroleum sectors to private management and
investment; and (iii) over time, supporting economic diversification through reform of the
investment climate and promotion of mining and irrigated agriculture. With respect to the food
price increases, as indicated in Box 1,the Government has taken a series of measures, including
a temporary lifting of the import tax on products of mass consumption and measures to relaunch
the 2008109 agricultural campaign through seed distribution. The Government is accompanied
in these reform efforts by the series of PRSCs and other IDA operations, and support from a
range of other development partners. The World Bank is also assisting with a US$5 million
nutrition project for small children and pregnant women approved by the Board on June 5,2008.

133.    Second, as a Sahelian economy, Burkina Faso is highly vulnerable to weather risk and
climate change. The current cotton campaign and subsistence agriculture has been negatively
affected by late rains. Efforts to improve competitiveness of the sector are hurt by reduced
output and farmers' rising indebtedness. Under the ongoing Country Economic Memorandum,
the World Bank is exploring the feasibility of developing weather risk transfer instruments to
help the authoritiesmitigating some of this risk in the future.

134.   Third, Burkina Faso's overall competitivenessis hurt by the strong appreciation of the
Euro against the US dollar, as the CFAF is pegged to the Euro. This is negatively affecting

revenue of cotton producers in CFAF terms and revenue mobilization for the budget, while real
appreciation of the domestic currency further hampers the economy's diversification by loss of
comparative advantage. To partly hedge against this risk, the World Bank will be providing
technical assistance to SOFITEX to develop a risk management strategy based on market
instrumentsand traditional techniques.

135.   Fourth, the implementation of the decentralization strategy poses significant
challenges. Officials working at the center may resist the transfer of responsibilities to local
Governments, while local Governments may not have sufficient resources and capacity to carry
out their new responsibilities. To address these challenges, the Government has adopted a
participatory and gradual decentralization approach that should ensure stakeholder consensus on
major issues, capacity building at the local level and fiscal sustainability over time. Several
donors support decentralization in Burkina, including IDA. This PRSC series accompanies
decentralization reforms in sectors which progress faster (education, water and sanitation) and
match delegationin competencies with genuine financialtransfers from the center.
